About This Guidebook

Purpose

This guidebook teaches you how to conduct systematic legal research within the Bangsamoro legal system. Whether you are preparing a legislative briefer, building a legal mandate matrix, drafting a bill, or analyzing a policy proposal, the quality of your output depends on the quality of your research. This guidebook gives you the methodology to get it right.

Who This Guidebook Is For

  • Parliament staff — legislative researchers at the Parliamentary Research and Legislative Support (PRLS), committee secretariat officers, and legislative staff in MP offices who prepare research memoranda, briefers, and legal analyses
  • MOA legal units — legal officers and planning staff in Ministries, Offices, and Agencies who need to establish legal mandates, verify authority, and ensure compliance
  • Consultants and advisors — policy consultants, legislative consultants, and governance practitioners working with any branch of the Bangsamoro Government
  • Anyone who needs to answer the question: What does the law say about this?

What You Will Learn

By the end of this guidebook, you will be able to:

  1. Formulate a legal research question that is answerable, specific, and tied to a legal outcome
  2. Navigate the seven-tier BARMM legal hierarchy and determine which tier controls for any given subject
  3. Search systematically through the Constitution, the BOL, BAAs, national laws, executive issuances, and jurisprudence
  4. Trace provision chains — following what one provision references to find all related authority
  5. Verify that every provision you cite is current, correctly attributed, and accurately quoted
  6. Produce five types of legal research output: Research Memoranda, Legal Analysis Memos, Comparative Matrices, Legal Mandate Matrices, and Legal Opinions
  7. Apply the Shari’ah research dimension whenever a question crosses the Shari’ah-secular boundary

How Legal Research Connects to Other Governance Competencies

Legal research is the foundation skill that feeds every other governance competency:

  • Bill drafting depends on legislative research (Stage 2 of the drafting process) — this guidebook expands that stage into a complete discipline.
  • Strategic planning depends on legal mandate verification — this guidebook teaches how to navigate the legal hierarchy that establishes those mandates.
  • Complete Staff Work depends on research (Step 3 of the CSW process) — this guidebook provides the methodology for that research.

If those competencies are the house, this guidebook is the foundation inspection manual.

AI Declaration

This guidebook was produced with AI assistance. The author used AI tools (Claude Code, Anthropic) for legal research compilation, first-draft generation, cross-referencing across the legislative archive, and fact-checking against known error patterns.

AI did not design the methodology. The five-step legal research process, the seven-tier BARMM legal hierarchy, the National Law Relationship Test, the provision chain trace technique, and every analytical framework in this guidebook come from the author’s professional experience in Bangsamoro governance — not from AI-generated suggestions. AI accelerated the writing. It did not direct the thinking.

Every legal provision cited in this guidebook was verified by the author against the source text. Every BOL article number was checked against the 18-article map. Every BAA number was checked against the enacted BAA index. The author bears full responsibility for the accuracy and completeness of the published content.

For the author’s full professional standards on AI-augmented governance, legislation, and development work — including the verification methodology, disclosure practices, and ethical principles that governed the production of this guidebook — see How to Use AI in Governance, Legislation, and Development Work in the Bangsamoro (Saidamen R. Mambayao, 2026).

How to Use This Guidebook

Read Chapters 1-3 first. They establish the methodology and the legal hierarchy that every other chapter builds on.

Then go to the chapter you need. If you are researching a BOL provision, go to Chapter 5. If you are searching for relevant BAAs, go to Chapters 6-7. If you need to trace a provision chain, go to Chapter 11. If you need to produce a specific output format, go to Chapter 12.

Use the appendices as working tools. The templates and checklists are designed to be detached, photocopied, or adapted for your office. The National Law Relationship Test (Appendix B) belongs on every researcher’s desk.

About the Author

Saidamen “Sayeed” R. Mambayao has worked in governance, legislation, and cooperative development across the Bangsamoro region for over a decade.

Before the establishment of the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region, he served as a Consultant of the Cooperative Development Authority under the former Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao, where he led programs on cooperative development, Islamic finance, and executive leadership. He also served as Secretariat Head and Program Director of the Bangsamoro Executives and Leaders League (BELL), Inc., and held leadership roles in the ARMM Islamic Finance Sub-Cluster and the Bangsamoro Coalition on Social Enterprise.

When the Bangsamoro Transition Authority was established in 2019, he led the development and implementation of two BARMM flagship programs: the Access to Higher and Modern Education (AHME) Scholarship Program under the Ministry of Basic, Higher, and Technical Education, and the Engaging the SK, Empowering the Youth (ESKEY) Program under the Ministry of the Interior and Local Government. He later served as Chief of Staff at the Office of a Member of Parliament, where he assisted on legislative portfolios covering women, youth, children, persons with disabilities, cooperatives, social enterprises, and halal industries. He drafted and reviewed legislative measures on scholarships, cultural heritage preservation, assistance to Other Bangsamoro Communities, and cooperative and social enterprise development. He also served as OIC-Legislative Affairs at the Office of MP Sittie Fahanie S. Uy-Oyod.

He is the Founder of MoroTech and MoroAcademy, social enterprises empowering Bangsamoro organizations and communities through knowledge and technology, and the Founding Chairperson of the Bangsamoro Scholars Association. He also founded the SEED Initiative, a consultancy and training firm focused on cooperative and social enterprise development. He is an Active Citizen and graduate of the British Council’s CSO-SEED Business and Investment Readiness Program.

Back to Table of Contents
01
Why Legal Research Matters in the Bangsamoro

Chapter 1 — Why Legal Research Matters in the Bangsamoro

A program without legal basis can be dismantled by a single COA audit finding. A bill that cites the wrong BOL article risks failing at committee. A policy recommendation that misattributes a mandate to the wrong ministry wastes months of institutional effort. Every one of these failures begins the same way: someone skipped the research, or did it badly.

Legal research is the discipline of finding, reading, tracing, and verifying the law that applies to a specific question. It is the foundation skill of the Bangsamoro Government. Whether you draft legislation, build strategic plans, prepare legislative briefers, write legal opinions, or advise a Minister, the quality of your work depends on the quality of your research. This guidebook teaches you how to do it right.

1.1 What Legal Research Is — and What It Is Not

Legal research is the systematic process of identifying all applicable legal authority for a specific question, verifying that authority is current and correctly understood, and organizing it for use by a decision-maker.

That definition has four parts. Each one matters.

Systematic. You do not browse the law and hope to find what you need. You work through a structured process — the five-step methodology in Chapter 2 — that ensures you check every relevant source in a defined order. A researcher who searches only the BOL and misses a controlling BAA has not completed the research.

All applicable legal authority. You are not looking for one provision that supports a predetermined conclusion. You are looking for every provision that bears on the question — including provisions that limit, contradict, or complicate the answer. A research memorandum that cites only favorable authority is advocacy, not research.

Verifying that authority is current. The Bangsamoro Parliament has enacted 89 Bangsamoro Autonomy Acts since 2019. National laws are amended. Executive orders are superseded. Supreme Court decisions redefine boundaries. A provision that was good law last year may have been repealed, amended, or declared unconstitutional. Verification is not optional — it is the final step of every research task.

Organized for use by a decision-maker. Raw legal research has no value until it reaches the person who needs it. The output must be structured, cited, and clear enough that a Member of Parliament, a Minister, or a legal officer can act on it without re-doing the research.

What Legal Research Is Not

Legal research is distinct from three other competencies that depend on it:

Legal research is not legal analysis. Research answers the question: What does the law say? Analysis answers the question: What does it mean for this situation? Research produces a Research Memorandum — a structured inventory of applicable authority. Analysis produces a Legal Opinion or IRAC Memo — an interpretation with a conclusion. You cannot analyze what you have not first researched. Chapter 12 covers both outputs and the pipeline between them.

Legal research is not legal review. Research finds the law. Review checks whether a document — a bill, a contract, a policy — complies with the law. A legal reviewer asks: Does this bill contradict the BOL? But to answer that question, the reviewer must first research what the BOL says about the subject. Research comes first.

Legal research is not bill drafting. Drafting is the act of writing legislation. It is a craft that requires its own set of skills — structure, style, precision, internal consistency. But Stage 2 of the drafting process — Legislative Research — is where the drafter must conduct the same systematic search this guidebook teaches. A well-drafted bill built on bad research is a well-built house on a cracked foundation.

1.2 The Cost of Getting It Wrong

Legal research errors are not academic mistakes. They have institutional consequences.

Laws declared unconstitutional. The Supreme Court has declared several Bangsamoro Autonomy Acts unconstitutional — underscoring the importance of thorough legal research before enactment.

BAA 58 and BAA 77 — the Parliament’s first and second attempts at establishing parliamentary districts — were both declared unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in October 2025. The Court found that the BTA exceeded its delegated authority, citing violations of constitutional provisions on representation, equitable apportionment, and the contiguity requirement. BAA 77 was also enacted after the election period commenced, breaching the Voters Registration Act. BAA 86, enacted in January 2026, established 32 parliamentary districts after accounting for the Sulu exclusion — the legislative response required precisely the kind of thorough, multi-tier research this guidebook teaches.

The Supreme Court also declared unconstitutional Section 5 of BAA 53, BAA 54, and BAA 55 — the municipality creation acts for three new towns in Maguindanao del Norte. The Court ruled that plebiscites must include qualified voters from both the new and mother municipalities, not only from the barangays comprising the new municipality. This was a constitutional requirement that thorough research across Tier 1 (Constitution) and Tier 7 (jurisprudence on plebiscite requirements) would have identified before enactment.

These cases illustrate the same lesson: incomplete research at any tier of the legal hierarchy increases the risk that enacted legislation will not survive judicial review.

Misattributed mandates and functions. A common error in MOA strategic plans is claiming authority for functions that no law assigns to the office. This happens when planning staff rely on institutional memory instead of reading the actual text of their creating law. During the transition from ARMM to BARMM, some functions may have been carried over by institutional practice without being formally assigned under BAA 13 or the BOL. When legal research is skipped, the gap between what a MOA does and what it is authorized to do goes undetected — until an audit, a budget review, or a parliamentary inquiry exposes it.

Possible COA disallowances. The Commission on Audit reviews whether government expenditures have legal basis. When a Ministry, Office, or Agency spends public funds on a program that lacks legal authority, COA issues a Notice of Disallowance. The responsible officers may be required to refund the amount. The root cause, in many cases, is a failure to verify that the MOA’s mandate actually covers the activity. A Legal Mandate Matrix built on thorough research would have caught the gap before the first peso was spent.

Wrong citations. The BOL has 18 articles (I-XVIII), and adjacent article numbers are easily confused — citing Article IX (Basic Rights) when the correct reference is Article X (Justice System), or attributing the Block Grant to Article XII, Section 9 when the actual provision is Article XII, Sections 15-16. Citation errors in any legal document — whether a research memorandum, a policy recommendation, or a legislative briefer — damage credibility and mislead decision-makers. Every citation is preventable through verification against the verbatim BOL text.

1.3 The Legal Research Ecosystem

Legal research in the Bangsamoro Government does not happen in isolation. It sits within an ecosystem of competencies that produce, consume, and verify legal knowledge.

Three Competencies, One Pipeline

The relationship between the three core legal competencies follows a pipeline:

  1. Research (find the law) → produces a Research Memorandum
  2. Analysis (interpret the law) → consumes the Research Memorandum, produces a Legal Opinion, IRAC Memo, or Legal Mandate Matrix
  3. Review (verify compliance) → consumes both, produces an Accuracy Report

The pipeline flows in one direction. You cannot analyze what you have not researched. You cannot review what you have not analyzed. And when the review finds an error, the correction loops back to research — re-reading the source, re-verifying the provision, re-checking the citation.

Who Does Legal Research?

Parliamentary Research and Legislative Support (PRLS). The Parliament’s research arm conducts policy and legal research to support committee work, legislative analysis, and plenary deliberations. PRLS produces Legislative Research Briefers and policy notes for the institutional record.

Legislative staff in MP offices. Each MP’s office has staff responsible for preparing legislative briefers, committee hearing preparation, and bill analysis. These staff conduct legal research as part of the bill drafting process (Stage 2) and the legislative briefer preparation process.

MOA legal officers. Every Ministry, Office, and Agency has a legal unit or legal officer responsible for reviewing contracts, advising on compliance, and preparing legal opinions. These officers conduct legal research to answer mandate questions, verify authority for proposed programs, and assess the applicability of national laws within BARMM.

Consultants and advisors. Policy consultants, legislative consultants, and governance practitioners conduct legal research as part of their advisory work — drafting bills, preparing policy recommendations, building strategic plans, and conducting institutional assessments.

You. If you are reading this guidebook, you are part of this ecosystem. The methodology you learn here applies regardless of your specific role.

Working with PRLS

If you work in an MP’s office, a committee secretariat, or a MOA, you will likely work with PRLS at some point. Two rules govern that relationship:

PRLS research supplements, it does not replace, your own. PRLS can provide background research, policy analysis, and legislative landscape reviews. But the drafter, the legislative researcher, or the legal officer is ultimately responsible for the accuracy of the final product. If PRLS provides a Research Memorandum, you must verify its findings against the source documents — not accept them on trust.

Credit PRLS when you use their work. If your Legislative Research Briefer or legal opinion incorporates findings from PRLS research, cite PRLS as a source. This is both an ethical obligation and a practical one — it creates an audit trail that allows the research to be verified later.

1.4 What Legal Research Produces

Legal research produces structured outputs. Each output has a specific format, a specific audience, and a specific purpose. Chapter 12 covers all five in detail. Here is a preview:

Output What It Contains Who Uses It When
Research Memorandum All applicable authorities organized by tier, with verbatim provisions and status The researcher and downstream consumers After every research task
Legal Analysis Memo (IRAC) Issue, Rule, Application, Conclusion — a structured legal argument MPs, Ministers, legal officers When a legal question needs interpretation
Comparative Legal Matrix Side-by-side comparison of provisions across multiple sources Committee staff, drafters When assessing how different laws treat the same subject
Legal Mandate Matrix Every legal provision granting authority to a specific MOA Planning officers, legal officers During strategic planning and mandate verification
Legal Opinion Formal interpretation with a recommendation Ministers, Executive Directors When an institutional decision requires legal guidance

The Research Memorandum is the foundation. Every other output depends on it. If the memorandum is incomplete — if it misses a tier, omits a relevant BAA, or cites a superseded provision — every downstream product inherits the error.

1.5 How to Read This Guidebook

Chapters 1-3 are the foundation. Read them in order. Chapter 1 (this chapter) frames the discipline. Chapter 2 teaches the five-step methodology. Chapter 3 teaches the seven-tier legal hierarchy and the National Law Relationship Test that resolves conflicts between tiers.

Chapters 4-10 apply the methodology to each tier of the hierarchy. Go to the chapter you need: Constitution (Chapter 4), BOL (Chapter 5), BAA 13 and charter BAAs (Chapter 6), other BAAs (Chapter 7), national laws (Chapter 8), Shari’ah and customary law (Chapter 9), jurisprudence (Chapter 10). Each chapter includes a worked example, a template, and a quality checklist.

Chapter 11 teaches provision chain tracing — the technique for following what one provision references until you have found all related authority. This is the skill that separates competent research from thorough research.

Chapter 12 covers the five output formats. If you know how to research but need to produce a specific deliverable, start here.

Chapter 13 covers verification, quality, and continuous learning. Read this before submitting any research product.

The Appendices are detachable working tools. Print them. Photocopy them. Keep the National Law Relationship Test (Appendix B) and the Research Quality Checklist (Appendix J) on your desk.

Every chapter includes:

  • Worked Example — a real research scenario walked through step by step
  • Common Pitfalls — the mistakes that experienced researchers still make
  • Template — a fill-in format you can use immediately
  • Quality Checklist — a verification tool to confirm your research is complete

Key Takeaways

  1. Legal research is a discipline, not a step. It is not “Stage 2 of drafting” or “Step 1 of planning.” It is the foundational competency that all other legal work depends on.

  2. Research answers “what does the law say?” — not “what should the law say?” Keep the boundary clear. Research is descriptive. Analysis is interpretive. Advocacy is prescriptive. This guidebook covers the first.

  3. The cost of bad research is institutional. Wrong citations, misattributed mandates, and unchecked provisions risk COA findings, failed legislation, and damaged credibility. Good research is cheaper than the alternative.

  4. The pipeline is one-directional. Research → Analysis → Review. You cannot skip or reverse the order.

  5. This guidebook is the foundation. Bill drafting, strategic planning, and Complete Staff Work all depend on the competency taught here.

Back to Table of Contents
02
The Legal Research Process: A Five-Step Methodology

Chapter 2 — The Legal Research Process: A Five-Step Methodology

You have a legal question. Maybe a Minister asks whether the MOA has authority to regulate a new activity. Maybe a committee chair needs to know what laws already cover a bill’s subject matter. Maybe a planning officer needs the legal basis for a proposed program. The question is clear. Now what?

This chapter gives you a structured process that works for any legal research question in the Bangsamoro context. It has five steps. Each step produces a specific output. Together, they transform a question into a Research Memorandum — the documented inventory of all applicable legal authority, verified and organized for use.

2.1 Overview: From Question to Research Memorandum

The five steps are:

  1. Issue Analysis — Frame the question. Identify the jurisdiction. Generate search terms.
  2. Landscape Review — Understand the legal and policy terrain before searching specific provisions.
  3. Systematic Source Search — Search each tier of the legal hierarchy methodically, building a Comparative Matrix as you go.
  4. Provision Chain Trace — Follow what provisions reference until you have found all related authority.
  5. Verification — Confirm every provision is current, correctly cited, and accurately quoted.

The steps are sequential in design but iterative in practice. Step 3 may reveal that your research question from Step 1 was too broad. Step 4 may send you back to Step 3 to search a tier you thought was irrelevant. Step 5 may reveal that a key provision was repealed, forcing you to re-trace the chain. This is normal. The structure keeps you oriented; the iteration keeps you thorough.

The output of the complete process is a Research Memorandum — a structured document listing every applicable authority by tier, with verbatim provisions, source paths, and verification status. Chapter 12 covers the full Research Memorandum format. This chapter teaches you how to produce the content that goes into it.

2.2 Step 1 — Issue Analysis

Issue analysis converts a vague request into a researchable question. This is the most neglected step. Researchers who skip it waste hours searching for provisions they do not need, or worse, miss provisions they do need because they searched with the wrong terms.

2.2.1 Formulating the Research Question

A good research question has three qualities:

Answerable. The question must be answerable by reading legal sources. “What is the best approach to IDP protection?” is a policy question — it requires judgment, not just research. “Does the Bangsamoro Parliament have authority to legislate on IDP protection?” is a legal research question — it can be answered by examining the BOL’s enumerated powers and relevant national laws.

Specific. The question identifies the legal issue precisely. “What laws apply to education in BARMM?” is too broad — there are dozens. “Does BAA 18 (Education Code) authorize the Ministry to regulate private madaris?” is specific enough to research efficiently.

Tied to a legal outcome. The question connects to a decision someone needs to make. “What does Article XII say about fiscal autonomy?” is descriptive but purposeless. “Can the Bangsamoro Government impose an excise tax on cement manufactured within the region?” ties the research to a concrete institutional decision.

Formulating Technique: The Three-Part Frame

For any research request, extract three elements:

Element Question Example
Subject What legal topic are you researching? Regulation of ride-hailing services
Jurisdiction BARMM only, national only, or both? Both — transport is among the BOL’s enumerated powers but national agencies also regulate
Action What does the requester want to know? Whether the Bangsamoro Parliament can enact a regional ride-hailing regulatory framework

The three-part frame produces a precise research question: Does the Bangsamoro Parliament have authority to enact a regional framework for regulating ride-hailing services, given existing national regulation under RA 11659 and the BOL’s enumerated powers?

Before reading further: take the last research request you received and apply the three-part frame. Does the reframed question change what you would search for?

2.2.2 Identifying the Jurisdiction

Every research question has a jurisdictional dimension. In the Bangsamoro context, three possibilities exist:

  1. Enumerated Bangsamoro power (no national law consistency required) — The subject falls within the 55 enumerated powers in BOL Article V, Section 2, and the BOL does not require consistency with national law. Example: administrative organization of regional MOAs.

  2. National law consistency required — The subject involves both Bangsamoro and national authority. Either the BOL explicitly requires national law consistency (civil service, budget, elections, audit, indigenous peoples’ rights), or the subject involves overlapping competencies. Example: procurement (RA 12009 applies directly; BOL Art. VII, Sec. 28 requires budget consistency with national laws).

  3. Residual national power — The subject involves powers not granted to the Bangsamoro Government (Art. V, Sec. 1). Example: national defense, foreign policy, citizenship.

Identifying the jurisdiction at the outset determines which tiers of the hierarchy you need to search. If the subject is an enumerated Bangsamoro power with no national law consistency required, you focus on Tiers 2-4 (BOL, BAA 13, other BAAs). If national law consistency is required, you must also cover Tier 5 (national laws) thoroughly. If the subject is a residual national power, the Bangsamoro Parliament has no legislative competence, and your research should confirm this boundary rather than look for authority that does not exist.

Chapter 3 covers the National Law Relationship Decision Matrix that resolves jurisdictional questions systematically.

AI in Practice

AI can help generate search terms by suggesting statutory synonyms and related concepts you might miss. Give it your research question and ask for alternative legal terms.

What AI does: Generates 15-20 search term variations including statutory language, broader concepts, and related subjects across Filipino, English, and Arabic. What you do: Select the terms that match your jurisdiction and subject matter — AI suggests, you decide which are relevant. Watch for: AI may suggest terms from national law that do not apply in BARMM, or use terminology from other jurisdictions. Always verify that suggested terms match BOL and BAA language.

2.2.3 Generating Search Terms

Legal provisions do not always use the terms you expect. Generate a search term list that includes:

  • Primary terms — the subject’s standard legal terminology. Example: “ride-hailing,” “transportation network company,” “TNC.”
  • Statutory synonyms — terms used in specific laws. Example: the BOL uses “cooperatives and social entrepreneurship” (Art. V, Sec. 2(k)), not “cooperative governance” or “coop regulation.”
  • Broader concepts — umbrella terms that might capture your subject. Example: “trade and industry,” “commerce,” “public convenience.”
  • Related subjects — adjacent topics that might intersect. Example: “labor” (driver employment), “consumer protection” (passenger rights), “data privacy” (rider data).

Write the search terms down. You will use them at every tier in Step 3.

2.3 Step 2 — Landscape Review

Before diving into specific provisions, understand the terrain. The landscape review is a quick scan of secondary sources that tells you what kind of legal environment you are entering.

2.3.1 Secondary Sources and Governance Context

Governance context. Invoke your understanding of BARMM’s institutional structure. Which MOA is responsible for this subject? Where does it sit in the organizational hierarchy? What are its mandated powers and functions under BAA 13? This context tells you where to look for sector-specific BAAs and executive issuances.

Existing research. Has anyone in the government already researched this question? Check: - PRLS policy notes and research papers - Committee reports from relevant parliamentary committees - Legal opinions issued by the Attorney-General’s Office (BAA 5) - Previous legislative research briefers on related bills

Commentary and doctrine. For subjects with significant jurisprudential history — autonomy law, constitutional limitations, Shari’ah jurisdiction — academic commentary and legal doctrine help you understand the landscape before searching for specific provisions.

The landscape review takes 30 minutes to an hour. It is not deep research — it is orientation. You are looking for landmarks, not destinations. The destinations come in Step 3.

2.3.2 Development Plan Context

If the research question relates to a policy or program, check the 2nd Bangsamoro Development Plan (BDP) 2023-2028. The BDP provides development context — which BDP goals and strategies relate to your subject, which chapters cover the sector, and what targets the Bangsamoro Government has committed to.

BDP context matters because: - A program that aligns with a BDP goal has stronger institutional support - The BDP may identify policy gaps that your research question touches on - BPDA evaluation of MOA strategic plans is based on BDP alignment

This step is optional for purely legal questions (“Does the BOL authorize X?”) but essential for mandate or policy questions (“What is the legal basis for this development program?”).

2.4 Step 3 — Systematic Source Search

This is the core of legal research. You work through each tier of the seven-tier BARMM legal hierarchy, searching for every provision that bears on your question. Chapters 4-7 cover each tier in detail. This section teaches the overall methodology.

AI in Practice

AI can search the entire legislative archive — 11,866 Republic Acts, 89 BAAs, 2,572 Executive Orders, 38,857 Supreme Court decisions — faster than any human researcher. It extracts provisions, builds comparative matrices, and traces cross-references across tiers simultaneously.

What AI does: Searches all seven tiers in parallel, extracts verbatim provisions, and builds the Applicable Authorities table and Comparative Matrix. What you do: Apply the National Law Relationship Test (Section 2.4.1) to determine which tier controls. Verify every provision AI extracts against the source text. Form all legal conclusions independently. Watch for: AI frequently cites wrong BOL article numbers (Art. IX instead of Art. X) and invents BAA numbers. Every citation must be verified against the source document — not accepted from AI output alone.

2.4.1 Working Through the Seven Tiers

Search in this order:

Tier Source What You Are Looking For
1 1987 Philippine Constitution Constitutional provisions relevant to the subject; constitutional constraints on Bangsamoro legislative authority
2 Bangsamoro Organic Law (RA 11054) Enumerated powers, general welfare clause, structural requirements, Shari’ah provisions, fiscal provisions
3 Bangsamoro Administrative Code (BAA 13) MOA mandates, powers and functions, organizational structure, governance framework
4 Other Bangsamoro Autonomy Acts Creating/charter BAAs, cross-cutting BAAs (17 civil service, 84 budget, 49 local governance), sector-specific BAAs
5 Applicable National Laws National RAs that apply directly, suppletorily, or through adoption; IRRs
6 Executive Issuances Chief Minister EOs, ministerial MCs, AOs, IRRs for specific BAAs
7 Jurisprudence Supreme Court decisions interpreting BARMM authority or the subject matter

The order matters. Higher tiers constrain lower tiers. If the Constitution prohibits something, no BOL provision can authorize it. If the BOL grants an enumerated power over a subject with no national law consistency requirement, national laws do not apply (except where the BOL requires consistency). Starting at the top ensures you understand the boundaries before searching the details.

Do not skip tiers. Even if you think a tier is irrelevant, scan it. Constitutional provisions in unexpected articles (Art. XIII on social justice, Art. XIV on education) often apply. National laws in unexpected domains (data privacy, anti-graft, women’s rights) often have BARMM implications. The five minutes you spend confirming a tier is irrelevant may save you from missing a controlling provision.

2.4.2 Recording What You Find

For each provision you find, record these fields:

Field What to Enter
Tier Which tier of the hierarchy (1-7)
Legal Source Full name and number (e.g., “Republic Act No. 11054”)
Provision Specific article, section, subsection (e.g., “Art. V, Sec. 2(oo)”)
Verbatim Text The exact text of the provision — copy from the source, do not paraphrase
Authority/Mandate Concise statement of what the provision authorizes, requires, or prohibits
Actor Who has the obligation or authority — the Bangsamoro Government, the National Government, a specific MOA, or both
Status In force / Amended by [law] / Repealed by [law] / Under review
Source Path Where you read this provision — file path, URL, or document reference

This recording discipline is non-negotiable. If you cannot point to the exact provision, the exact text, and the exact source where you read it, your research is unverifiable. Paraphrases drift. Verbatim citations do not.

2.4.3 Building the Comparative Matrix as You Search

As you work through the seven tiers, you will often find that multiple sources address the same subject differently. The Constitution may establish a broad principle. The BOL may grant specific power. A national law may impose requirements. A BAA may operationalize the power. These provisions do not just coexist — they interact, sometimes in conflict.

The Comparative Matrix is the tool that makes these interactions visible. You build it during the search, not after. Each time you find a provision at a new tier that addresses the same subject, you add a column to the matrix.

Structure:

AspectConstitutionBOL (RA 11054)National LawBAAConflict / Gap
Authority granted[provision text][provision text][provision text][provision text][Note conflicts]
Scope / coverage
Who is responsible
Limitations
Penalties / enforcement
Status

How to use it:

  1. Start at Tier 1. When you find a constitutional provision relevant to your subject, enter it in the Constitution column for the applicable row (authority, scope, limitations, etc.).

  2. Add each tier as you search. When you reach Tier 2 and find BOL provisions, fill in the BOL column. When you reach Tiers 3-4 and find BAAs, fill in the BAA column. National laws go in the National Law column.

  3. Fill the Conflict/Gap column as differences appear. The matrix reveals:

  4. Conflicts — where two tiers say different things about the same subject. Example: a national law requires registration with a national agency, but a BAA assigns registration to a regional agency.
  5. Gaps — where a tier is silent on an aspect that another tier addresses. Example: the BOL grants power over transportation, but no BAA has been enacted to operationalize it.
  6. Suppletory relationships — where a national law applies because BARMM legislation has not yet been enacted. Example: RA 12009 (New Government Procurement Act) applies directly in BARMM because no Bangsamoro procurement BAA has been enacted to date.

  7. Evolution — how the legal treatment of a subject has changed over time (from ARMM-era law to BTA-era legislation).

  8. Use the matrix to identify which tier controls. The Conflict/Gap column feeds directly into the national law relationship analysis (Chapter 3). When you find a conflict, you apply the national law relationship test: Is this an enumerated power with no national law consistency requirement? Does the BOL require national law consistency? The matrix gives you the raw material; the national law relationship analysis gives you the answer.

Why build it during the search, not after?

Three reasons.

First, the matrix guides your search. When you fill in the Constitution column and see that the Constitution imposes a limitation (say, “no money shall be paid out of the treasury except in pursuance of an appropriation made by law” — Art. VI, Sec. 29), you know to look for the appropriation provisions at every subsequent tier. The matrix tells you what to search for next.

Second, the matrix catches conflicts early. If you build it after the search, you may not notice that a BAA contradicts a BOL provision until you compile the results. If you build it during the search, you catch the conflict the moment you read the BAA — and you can immediately trace the conflict to determine which provision controls.

Third, the matrix becomes a deliverable. When completed, the Comparative Matrix is one of the five standard legal research outputs (Chapter 12). Building it during the search means you produce a deliverable as a byproduct of the process, rather than as an extra step at the end.

The matrix does not need to cover every aspect for every question. If your research question is narrow — “Can BARMM impose an excise tax on locally manufactured goods?” — the matrix might have only two rows (authority granted, limitations). If the question is broad — “What is the legal framework for cooperative regulation in BARMM?” — the matrix might have six or eight rows. Scale it to the question.

Two Types of Comparative Matrix

The tier-by-tier matrix above compares how different levels of the legal hierarchy treat the same subject. There is a second type that compares how different laws at the same level treat the same subject.

The Verbatim Section-by-Section Matrix compares multiple bills or laws provision by provision. This is the format used in committee work when the Parliament has several competing bills on the same topic. It goes beyond passive comparison — it is a decision-support tool that produces actionable consolidation recommendations.

The OOBC’s Verbatim Matrix on three OBC bills (394, 205, 203) demonstrates the full format.1 It uses six columns:

ColumnPurpose
Section # & TitleStandardized row labels that normalize across bills with different numbering schemes
Bill 394Verbatim provision text (or [No corresponding provision] when absent)
Bill 205Verbatim provision text
Bill 203Verbatim provision text
Analysis & CommentsIdentifies critical divergences, institutional impacts, unique strengths, and gaps across the bills
Position / ProposalConcrete recommendations: which bill's approach to adopt, what to incorporate from others, how to consolidate

The matrix compared 18 sections across all three bills — from short title through effectivity — and revealed three categories of findings:

  • Critical divergences: Bill 394 creates a new 21-member Bangsamoro Development Assistance and Management Council (BDAMC) chaired by the Chief Minister, fundamentally altering OOBC’s institutional position from “lead agency” (Bills 205/203) to “technical secretariat.” The Analysis column flagged this as “CRITICAL DIVERGENCE” and the Position column recommended a hybrid governance model.
  • Coverage gaps: Only Bill 394 had explicit prioritization criteria and community determination procedures (BPA/BPC/SDA classification). Neither Bill 205 nor 203 provided comparable targeting mechanisms. The Position column recommended adopting Bill 394’s framework with enhancements.
  • Unique provisions: Bill 203’s Peace and Culture Centers (physical infrastructure for OBC service delivery) and co-management model with OBC organizations had no equivalent in Bills 394 or 205. The Position column recommended incorporating this into Bill 394’s BDAP framework.

This matrix format is the standard for parliamentary committee consolidation work. When a committee receives multiple bills on the same topic, the verbatim matrix is how the committee secretariat presents the options to the committee chair.

When to use which:

Matrix Type When to Use What It Reveals
Tier-by-tier (hierarchy) Researching a legal question across the hierarchy Conflicts between tiers, gaps, suppletory relationships
Section-by-section (verbatim) Comparing multiple bills or laws on the same topic Structural differences, coverage gaps, consolidation opportunities

Both types are standard legal research outputs. Chapter 12 covers the formal templates for each. Appendix E provides the annotated Comparative Legal Matrix template.

2.4.4 The Shari’ah Checkpoint

At the end of Step 3, before moving to Step 4, ask: Does this research question have a Shari’ah dimension?

A Shari’ah dimension exists when: - The subject involves personal law matters (marriage, divorce, inheritance, custody, family relations) for Muslims - The subject involves property relations between Muslims - The subject involves minor criminal offenses that fall under Shari’ah court jurisdiction - The subject intersects with Islamic finance, halal certification, waqf (endowment), or zakat (alms) - A provision references “Shari’ah,” “Islamic law,” or “Muslim personal law”

If yes, you need to search Chapter 9’s Shari’ah research sources in addition to the seven tiers. Add a Shari’ah column to your Comparative Matrix.

If no, note it in your Search Record (“Shari’ah dimension: None identified”) and proceed.

2.5 Step 4 — Provision Chain Trace

Step 3 finds the provisions that directly address your question. Step 4 follows what those provisions reference — tracing the chain of authority, rights, obligations, and definitions across multiple sources until you have found all related authority.

This is the step that separates surface-level research from thorough research. Chapter 11 covers the full methodology. Here is the overview.

Why chain tracing matters. A provision rarely stands alone. BOL Article VI, Section 12 says the Bangsamoro Government shall “provide assistance to enhance their economic, social, and cultural development” for communities outside the region. But what does “economic, social, and cultural development” mean? To answer that, you must trace through Article IX (Basic Rights), Article XIII (Regional Economy), and the relevant sections of BAA 13. Without the trace, your research is grounded in one provision. With it, your research maps dozens of interconnected provisions.

The five-step trace:

  1. Extract traceable concepts — from each provision found in Step 3, identify terms that point to other provisions: “rights,” “powers,” “functions,” “in coordination with,” named populations, named institutions.

  2. Full-source scan — read the entire relevant source document (not just keyword matches). For BOL provisions, read all five chapter files. For BAA 13, read the relevant Books.

  3. Relevance filter — given your specific research question, determine which traced provisions actually apply. Not every “rights” provision is relevant to every question.

  4. Depth limit — follow chains up to three levels deep. Stop when the concept is fully defined with verbatim text, three levels of chaining have been reached, or no further references exist.

  5. Output as Provision Chain Map — record each chain in a structured table:

Source Provision Traceable Concept Related Provisions Found Relevance to Research Question

The Provision Chain Map becomes a section of your Research Memorandum.

2.6 Step 5 — Verification

Every provision you cite must be verified. Verification answers three questions:

Is it current? Has the provision been repealed, amended, or superseded by later legislation? The Bangsamoro Parliament passes new BAAs regularly. National laws are amended. Executive issuances expire. A provision that was good law last year may not be good law today.

Is it correctly cited? Does the article number, section number, and subsection match the source? The BOL has 18 articles with adjacent numbers that are easily confused — Art. IX (Basic Rights) cited when Art. X (Justice System) is correct, Art. XII Sec. 9 cited when Secs. 15-16 is correct. Verify every citation against the verbatim source text.

Is it accurately quoted? Does the text in your Research Memorandum match the source word-for-word? Paraphrases introduce errors. “Shall take into consideration” is not the same as “shall ensure the protection.” Copy the text from the source. Do not retype from memory.

Verification Checklist

For each provision in your Research Memorandum:

  • ☐ Read the source file or document containing the provision (not from memory — actually read it)
  • ☐ Confirm the article, section, and subsection numbers match
  • ☐ Confirm the quoted text is verbatim
  • ☐ Check whether the provision has been amended by a later BAA, national law, or executive issuance
  • ☐ Check whether the provision has been the subject of a Supreme Court ruling
  • ☐ Record the verification source path (where you read it)
  • ☐ Mark status: In force / Amended by [law] / Repealed / Under review

Source Hierarchy for Verification

Verify provisions in this order of reliability:

  1. Verbatim source texts — the full text of the BOL (RA 11054), all 89 enacted BAAs, and the Constitution. Always verify provisions against the source text, not from memory or secondary summaries.
  2. Original PDF source documents — authoritative but slower to search
  3. Official Gazette — for national laws and recent BAAs not yet transcribed
  4. Web sources — LawPhil, SC E-Library. Label as [Source: web — verify against official copy]

Never cite a provision you have not read in the source. Never verify from memory.

2.7 The Search Record

Maintain a Search Record alongside your Research Memorandum. The Search Record documents how you searched — not just what you found. It serves two purposes: reproducibility (someone else can repeat your search) and completeness audit (you can verify you searched all relevant sources).

The Search Record should include:

Field What to Record
Research question The formulated question from Step 1
Search terms used The complete list from Step 1, including terms that yielded no results
Tiers searched Which of the 7 tiers you covered, and confirmation of coverage
Sources searched Specific files, databases, websites, and documents consulted
Provisions found Count of provisions recorded in the Research Memorandum
Shari’ah dimension Identified / None identified
Chains traced Count of provision chains followed in Step 4
Verification status All verified / [N] pending verification
Date When the research was completed
Researcher Your name and position

The Search Record is an appendix to your Research Memorandum. It does not need to be long — half a page is typical. But it must be honest. If you did not search Tier 7 (jurisprudence), say so. A known gap is better than an unacknowledged one.

2.8 When to Stop Researching

Research can expand indefinitely. There is always one more provision to check, one more chain to trace, one more national law that might apply. You need a stopping rule.

Stop when:

  1. All seven tiers have been searched for your specific question (or you have documented why a tier was excluded).
  2. All provision chains have been traced to their logical endpoints or the three-level depth limit.
  3. All provisions have been verified as current.
  4. Your Comparative Matrix shows no unresolved conflicts — every conflict has been classified using the national law relationship test (Chapter 3).
  5. Your research question has been answered. You can state, with citations, what the law says about the subject.

Do not stop when:

  • You have found one supporting provision but have not checked for contradicting ones
  • You have searched some tiers but not all
  • You have unverified provisions in your memorandum
  • You have an open Conflict/Gap cell in your Comparative Matrix that you have not investigated

2.9 Worked Example

Research question: Does the Bangsamoro Parliament have authority to enact a regional cooperative code?

Step 1: Issue Analysis

Element Detail
Subject Regulation of cooperatives within BARMM
Jurisdiction Enumerated power — BOL Art. V, Sec. 2(k) lists “Cooperatives and social entrepreneurship”; no national law consistency required
Action Determine whether Parliament can enact a Bangsamoro Cooperative Code, and what law currently governs
Search terms “cooperatives,” “social entrepreneurship,” “cooperative development,” “cooperative code,” “CDA,” “social enterprise”

Step 2: Landscape Review

  • Governance context: Cooperative development may fall under MTIT (trade, investments, tourism) per BAA 13. The BOL mandates the Bangsamoro Government to “recognize, promote, protect, and support the development of cooperatives” and to enact “a Bangsamoro cooperative code” (Art. XIII, Sec. 24).
  • National context: RA 9520 (Philippine Cooperative Code of 2008) is the national cooperative framework. The Cooperative Development Authority (CDA) regulates cooperatives nationally.
  • BDP context: BDP Ch. 7 (Economy) covers cooperative development as part of economic strategy.

Step 3: Systematic Source Search (with Comparative Matrix)

Tier 1 — Constitution: - Art. XII, Sec. 15: “The Congress shall create an agency to promote the viability and growth of cooperatives.” Establishes national cooperative framework.

Tier 2 — BOL: - Art. V, Sec. 2(k): Bangsamoro Government has enumerated power over “Cooperatives and social entrepreneurship” (no national law consistency required). - Art. V, Sec. 3: General welfare clause — implied powers for cooperative regulation. - Art. XIII, Sec. 24: “The Bangsamoro Government… shall recognize, promote, protect, and support the development of cooperatives and other medium, small, and micro enterprises. It shall adopt and implement cooperative development policies and programs through the enactment of a Bangsamoro cooperative code.”

Tier 3 — BAA 13: - MTIT mandate includes trade, investments, and tourism — cooperatives may fall under this ministry.

Tier 4 — BAAs: - No BAA specifically codifying cooperative law has been enacted as of March 2026. This is a legislative gap.

Tier 5 — National Laws: - RA 9520 (Philippine Cooperative Code of 2008) — national cooperative framework; applies suppletorily in absence of a BAA.

Comparative Matrix (partial):

AspectConstitutionBOLNational LawConflict / Gap
AuthorityArt. XII, Sec. 15: Congress creates agency for cooperativesArt. V, Sec. 2(k): enumerated power over cooperatives (no national law consistency required); Art. XIII, Sec. 24: favorable environment mandateRA 9520: Philippine Cooperative Code; CDA regulatory authorityBOL grants enumerated power with no national law consistency requirement. RA 9520 applies suppletorily until Parliament acts.
ScopeNational"Within the Bangsamoro" — territorial limitationNational — applies to all cooperatives in the PhilippinesBARMM-registered cooperatives currently governed by RA 9520 (legislative gap)
Who regulatesCDA (national)No BARMM cooperative regulatory body establishedCDAGap: No BAA creates a regional cooperative authority or code. CDA continues to regulate.
BAA enactedN/AN/AN/ALegislative gap: Enumerated power exists but has not been exercised.

Step 4: Provision Chain Trace

Starting from BOL Art. V, Sec. 2(k), the traceable concept is “cooperatives and social entrepreneurship.” Tracing through: - Art. XIII, Sec. 24: Affirmative mandate — “favorable environment for cooperatives” - Art. XIII, Sec. 27: Social enterprises recognized alongside cooperatives - BAA 13 (MTIT mandate): What specific cooperative functions does MTIT have?

Finding: The BOL does not require national law consistency for cooperatives — unlike civil service (Art. VII, Sec. 39) or budget (Art. VII, Sec. 28). Cooperatives is an enumerated power with no national law consistency requirement, where a future BAA would govern.

Step 5: Verification

All provisions verified against the verbatim BOL text: - Art. V, Sec. 2(k): Verified — Republic Act No. 11054, Article V, Section 2(k) - Art. XIII, Sec. 24: Verified — Republic Act No. 11054, Article XIII, Section 24 - RA 9520: Verified — Republic Act No. 9520 (Philippine Cooperative Code of 2008) - BAA index: No cooperative BAA exists (confirmed against the enacted BAA index) - Status: All in force

Research Answer

The Bangsamoro Parliament has explicit enumerated authority under BOL Art. V, Sec. 2(k) to regulate cooperatives, with no national law consistency requirement. However, no BAA has been enacted to exercise this power. RA 9520 (Philippine Cooperative Code) applies suppletorily until Parliament enacts a Bangsamoro Cooperative Code. The researcher should note this as a legislative gap — the power exists, the mandate is clear (Art. XIII, Sec. 24), but Parliament has not yet acted.

2.10 Common Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Starting with the answer. If you begin research knowing what conclusion you want to reach, you will find only supporting provisions and ignore contradicting ones. Research answers “what does the law say?” — not “does the law support my position?” Search all seven tiers before forming a conclusion.

Pitfall 2: Paraphrasing from memory. You read a provision yesterday and think you remember what it says. You write it into your memorandum without re-reading the source. The paraphrase drifts — “shall ensure” becomes “shall promote,” “Bangsamoro Government” becomes “regional government,” “Section 12” becomes “Section 9.” Verify every provision against the source text every time.

Pitfall 3: Searching only the obvious tier. A researcher looking for BARMM authority over education goes straight to BAA 18 (Education Code) and stops. But the complete picture requires Tier 1 (Constitution Art. XIV on education), Tier 2 (BOL Art. V, Sec. 2(v) on education and skills training, plus Art. IX on basic rights including education), Tier 5 (national education laws that apply suppletorily), and possibly Tier 7 (SC decisions on educational autonomy). One BAA is not the whole answer.

Pitfall 4: Ignoring the actor. A provision says “The National Government shall ensure the protection of the rights of Bangsamoro people outside the region.” The researcher attributes this obligation to the Bangsamoro Government. The actor matters. Misattributing an obligation is a serious error — it creates a legal basis that does not exist.2

Pitfall 5: Skipping the Comparative Matrix. Searching tier by tier without comparing how different tiers treat the same subject means you miss conflicts, gaps, and suppletory relationships. The matrix catches what sequential reading misses.

Pitfall 6: Not recording the search. Six months later, a committee member asks “did you check whether any national law applies?” If you have no Search Record, you cannot answer. Record your search process — not just your findings.

2.11 Quality Checklist

Before submitting your Research Memorandum, confirm:

  • ☐ The research question is documented (three-part frame: subject, jurisdiction, action)
  • ☐ All seven tiers of the legal hierarchy have been searched (or exclusions documented)
  • ☐ Every provision is recorded with: tier, source, provision number, verbatim text, authority/mandate, actor, status, source path
  • ☐ A Comparative Matrix has been built for subjects addressed by multiple tiers
  • ☐ The Shari’ah checkpoint has been completed (dimension identified or confirmed absent)
  • ☐ Provision chains have been traced for all key provisions (up to 3 levels)
  • ☐ Every provision has been verified against the source text (not from memory)
  • ☐ No provision is cited that has been repealed, amended, or superseded without noting the change
  • ☐ The Search Record is complete: terms, tiers, sources, date, researcher
  • ☐ The Research Memorandum is organized by tier, with verbatim text and full citations
  • ☐ The Comparative Matrix conflicts and gaps have been classified using the national law relationship test (Chapter 3)

  1. VERBATIM MATRIX: Comparative Analysis and Recommendations on OBC Bills — Office for Other Bangsamoro Communities (OOBC), Office of the Chief Minister, Bangsamoro Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao (February 2026). This 20-page matrix compared the verbatim text of all 18 sections across Bills 394, 205, and 203 addressing Bangsamoro communities outside the region, with analysis and consolidation recommendations for each section. ↩

  2. Republic Act No. 11054, Article VI, Section 12. The provision assigns the primary protection obligation to the National Government, with the Bangsamoro Government’s role being coordination and assistance. ↩

03
The BARMM Legal Hierarchy: A Researcher's Guide

Chapter 3 — The BARMM Legal Hierarchy: A Researcher’s Guide

Different approaches to presenting the BARMM legal hierarchy exist — some place national laws at Tier 3 (above BAAs), others separate the Administrative Code (BAA 13) as its own tier and place national laws below other BAAs. Both approaches have merit, and both are incomplete, because the hierarchy is not a fixed ladder. It is a context-dependent structure where the order changes based on the subject matter.

This chapter gives you the definitive model: seven tiers with three relationships to national law that determine which tier controls for any given research question. Master this framework and you will never again ask “which law prevails?” without being able to answer it.

3.1 The Seven Tiers

The BARMM legal system draws authority from seven categories of legal sources, listed here from highest to lowest in their default order of precedence:

Tier Source Authority
1 1987 Philippine Constitution Supreme law; no other source may contradict it
2 Bangsamoro Organic Law (RA 11054) Organic act; defines BARMM powers and structure
3 Bangsamoro Administrative Code (BAA No. 13) Organizational structure and governance framework
4 Other Bangsamoro Autonomy Acts Sector-specific legislation enacted by Parliament
5 Applicable National Laws National RAs that apply within BARMM
6 Executive Issuances EOs, IRRs, MCs, AOs from CM or ministries
7 Jurisprudence Supreme Court decisions interpreting BARMM authority

Two things about this table require explanation.

First, the word “default.” Tiers 1, 2, 6, and 7 always occupy their positions. The Constitution always prevails. The BOL always prevails over BAAs. Executive issuances always yield to the BAAs they implement. Jurisprudence always provides binding interpretation. But the relationship between Tiers 3-5 — BAA 13, other BAAs, and national laws — depends on the subject matter. This is where the three relationships to national law come in (Section 3.2).

Second, BAA 13 gets its own tier. Some approaches group BAA 13 with other BAAs. This guidebook separates it as Tier 3 for a practical reason: BAA 13 (the Bangsamoro Administrative Code) is not just another BAA. It prescribes the structural, functional, and procedural principles of the entire Bangsamoro Government.1 It establishes all 15 ministries, defines the powers of the Chief Minister, creates BPDA and BEDC, and provides the governance framework within which all other BAAs operate. For the legal researcher, BAA 13 is the first place to look for institutional mandates and organizational authority — before sector-specific BAAs, before national laws. It is the government’s operating manual.

3.2 Three Relationships to National Law

The BOL does not create a simple hierarchy where Bangsamoro law always prevails over national law (or vice versa). Instead, it establishes three different relationships between Bangsamoro legislation and national law, and the hierarchy changes depending on which relationship applies to the research question.

3.2.1 No National Law Consistency Required

Definition: Subjects where the BOL enumerates the power under Article V, Section 2 and is silent on national law. In these areas, the Parliament has primary legislative competence. BAAs govern, and national law applies suppletorily only where no BAA has been enacted (legislative gap).

The 55 Enumerated Powers. Article V, Section 2 lists 55 subjects over which the Bangsamoro Government exercises authority:2

Subject to Section 20, Article X of the Constitution and this Organic Law, the Bangsamoro Government shall exercise its authority over the following matters without prejudice to the general supervision of the President of the Republic of the Philippines:

(a) Administration of justice; (b) Administrative organization; (c) Agriculture, livestock, and food security; (d) Ancestral domain and natural resources; … (ccc) Water supply and services, flood control, and irrigation systems.

The full list runs from (a) to (ccc) — 55 areas covering everything from administration of justice to water supply. Appendix A provides the complete enumeration.

What this means for research: When your research question falls within one of the 55 enumerated powers and the BOL is silent on national law, BAAs are the controlling authority. If Parliament has enacted a BAA on the subject, that BAA governs within BARMM. If no BAA has been enacted, the power exists but has not been exercised — you have identified a legislative gap (which may be significant for your research).

The general welfare clause. Article V, Section 3 extends the Bangsamoro Government’s authority beyond the literal text of the 55 enumerated powers:3

The Bangsamoro Government shall exercise the powers expressly granted, those necessarily implied therefrom, as well as powers necessary, appropriate, or incidental for its efficient and effective governance and those which are essential to the promotion of general welfare.

This means the Bangsamoro Government may also exercise implied powers — those necessarily implied from the express grants — and incidental powers — those necessary for efficient governance and general welfare. However, implied powers must be traceable to an express grant. You cannot use the general welfare clause to claim authority that no enumerated power supports.

3.2.2 National Law Consistency Required: The Five Types of BOL Conformity Language

Definition: Subjects where the BOL explicitly requires consistency with national law. The BOL enumerates the power but also specifies — using one of five distinct types of conformity language — that the Bangsamoro Government must act within national law boundaries. In these areas, national law sets the framework and BAAs must be consistent with it.

The BOL contains 126 constraining provisions scattered across all 18 articles — not a single list. An exhaustive scan reveals five types of conformity language, each with different legal effects:4

Type 1: “Shall Prevail” — Supremacy Clauses (11 provisions)

National law overrides any conflicting BAA. These are the strongest constraints — express conflict-resolution clauses.

BOL Provision Subject Verbatim Language
Art. VII, Sec. 39 Civil service “In case of conflict with the national laws, the Constitution and existing national civil service laws, rules, and regulations shall prevail“
Art. V, Sec. 1 Residual powers Powers not granted “shall be vested in the National Government”
Art. XII, Sec. 9 Tax limitations 12 categories of taxes BARMM cannot impose
Art. XVII, Sec. 1 BOL amendment Only Congress can amend the BOL
Art. XIII, Sec. 39 Telecom franchises Congress retains national franchise power

Civil service (Art. VII, Sec. 39) is the most constrained of all 55 powers — it is the only provision with an express conflict-resolution supremacy clause covering BARMM’s own legislation.5

Type 2: “Shall Conform” / Minimum Standards (7 provisions)

BARMM must meet or exceed national minimums. Can go above, cannot go below.

BOL Provision Subject Verbatim Language National Standard
Art. IX, Sec. 16 Basic education “shall conform to the minimum standards set by the National Government” DepEd K-12
Art. IX, Sec. 17 Vocational/TESD same language TESDA Training Regulations
Art. X, Sec. 8 Shari’ah judges “regular member of the Philippine Bar” SC Bar admission
Art. VII, Sec. 13 Compensation “shall adopt the Revised Compensation and Position Classification System…RA 6758” Salary Standardization
Art. XII, Sec. 3 Local treasurer qualifications “guided by the standards set by DOF-BLGF” DOF-BLGF standards
Art. VI, Sec. 10 Municipality creation “criteria laid down in RA 7160…shall be satisfied” LGC criteria
Art. XII, Sec. 20(b) Salary ceiling “No official…entitled to a salary rate higher than the maximum fixed…by applicable laws” RA 6758 grades

Type 3: “Consistent With” — Framework Alignment (33 provisions)

BAAs must not contradict national law but have room for regional adaptation within the framework.

BOL Provision Subject Key Language
Art. VII, Sec. 28 Budget “consistent with existing laws, rules and regulations of the National Government”6
Art. IV, Sec. 4 Elections “consistent with national election laws”7
Art. XIII, Sec. 32 Fishery “consistent with RA 8550”
Art. XIII, Sec. 26 Agriculture “consistent with RA 8550 and other national laws”
Art. XI, Sec. 5 DRRM “consistent with RA 10121”

This is the largest category. The full list of 33 provisions spans fiscal autonomy, natural resources, trade, and regulatory subjects. The researcher must search each relevant BOL article for “consistent with” language before concluding that no national law consistency is required.

Type 4: Institutional Jurisdiction Retention (47 provisions)

National agencies keep authority within BARMM. Cannot be displaced by BAAs.

Key retentions: Supreme Court (courts, bar, judicial personnel), COA (exclusive auditor — Art. XII, Sec. 2), CSC (civil service), COMELEC (elections), BSP (Islamic banking supervision), PNP/BFP/BJMP (security forces), DOE (fossil fuels — joint exercise), CHED/TESDA/DepEd (education coordination), FDA (food/drug/tobacco regulation), PSA (civil registration).

Type 5: “Shall Not Diminish” — One-Way Ratchet Floors (8 provisions)

Existing rights and privileges are the floor — can only go up, never down.

BOL Provision Subject What It Protects
Art. VI, Sec. 10 LGU privileges Privileges under RA 7160 “shall not be diminished”
Art. IX, Sec. 3 Indigenous peoples’ rights Rights under RA 8371 “shall not in any manner diminish”8
Art. XII, Sec. 8 Investor incentives Existing incentives honored
Art. XII, Sec. 36 IP revenue share IP community revenue share rights

The judicial test. The Supreme Court confirmed the enforceability of these constraints in Ali et al. v. BTA Parliament (G.R. No. E-02219, September 30, 2025): “While Congress may establish autonomous regions and grant them legislative authority, such authority must always operate within the bounds of the Constitution and national statutes.” The Court described this limitation as “clear and categorical.”9

What this means for research: When your research question falls into one of these areas, you must search both the BAA framework and the applicable national law. The type of conformity language determines the constraint’s strength: “shall prevail” is the strongest (national law controls absolutely), “shall conform” sets a minimum floor, “consistent with” requires framework alignment, institutional retention prevents displacement, and “shall not diminish” locks existing rights as a one-way ratchet.

Your Comparative Matrix (Chapter 2, Section 2.4.3) is essential here — it reveals where the BAA and the national law diverge, and the type of conformity language tells you whether that divergence is permissible.

Critical nuance: These five types of conformity language are the only areas where the BOL expressly constrains Bangsamoro legislation against national law. In all other enumerated powers where the BOL is silent on national law applicability (approximately 20 of the 55 powers — cooperatives, housing, culture, solid waste, humanitarian services, customary laws, etc.), the Parliament has primary legislative competence. Article VII, Section 39’s “national civil service laws shall prevail” applies only to civil service law — it is not a blanket rule that national law always prevails over BAAs.

3.2.3 Residual National Power

Definition: Subjects not enumerated in Article V, Section 2. The National Government retains authority because the power has not been granted to the Bangsamoro Government. Under Article V, Section 1:10

All powers, functions, and responsibilities not granted by the Constitution or by national law to the Bangsamoro Government shall be vested in the National Government.

This is the residual clause. It means the default is national authority. Only the powers expressly granted to BARMM (the 55 enumerated powers) and those necessarily implied from them fall within Bangsamoro legislative competence. Everything else — national defense, foreign policy, citizenship, immigration, customs, currency — remains with the National Government.

What this means for research: If your research question concerns a subject not among the 55 enumerated powers and not necessarily implied from them, the Bangsamoro Parliament has no legislative competence. Your research should confirm this boundary rather than look for authority that does not exist. This is a finding — the research answer is: The Bangsamoro Government does not have legislative competence over this subject.

3.3 The National Law Relationship Test

For any research question, apply this three-step test to determine which tier controls:

Step 1: Is the subject within the 55 enumerated powers (Art. V, Sec. 2)?

Read the full list of 55 powers (Appendix A). Does your subject match one of them — either directly or through the general welfare clause (Art. V, Sec. 3)?

  • YES → Proceed to Step 2.
  • NO → This is a residual national power. National law governs. The Bangsamoro Parliament has no legislative competence. Stop here.

Step 2: Does the BOL contain conformity language for this subject?

Check the five types of conformity language listed in Section 3.2.2 above. Does the BOL contain a “shall prevail,” “shall conform,” “consistent with,” institutional retention, or “shall not diminish” provision for this subject?

  • YES → National law consistency is required. Identify which type(s) of conformity language apply — this determines the constraint’s strength. Both BAAs and national laws apply. Build your Comparative Matrix.
  • NO → Proceed to Step 3.

Step 3: Has Parliament enacted a BAA on this subject?

Search the BAA archive (Tier 4). Has Parliament exercised its power by enacting legislation?

  • YES → The BAA governs. No national law consistency is required for this subject (though national laws of general application — anti-graft, data privacy, women’s rights — may still apply as cross-cutting obligations).
  • NO → The power exists but has not been exercised. This is a legislative gap. In the interim, national laws may apply suppletorily until Parliament acts.

The Flowchart

START: What is the subject of the research question?
    |
