Back to Table of Contents
05
The Bangsamoro Organic Law (RA 11054)

Chapter 5 — The Bangsamoro Organic Law (RA 11054)

The Bangsamoro Organic Law (RA 11054) is the instrument that created the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region, defined its powers, and established its government. It is Tier 2 of the BARMM legal hierarchy — subordinate only to the Constitution (Chapter 4). Every power the Bangsamoro Government exercises traces back to this organic act.

This chapter teaches you how to navigate the BOL’s 18-article structure without error, how to work with the 55 enumerated powers, the general welfare clause, and the residual clause, how to distinguish between explicit and implied authority, how to trace BOL provisions to their constitutional basis, and how to identify when the BOL delegates subjects to Parliament for further legislation. These are skills you will use in every research task.

5.1 Tier 2 — The Bangsamoro Organic Law (RA 11054)

5.1.1 The 18-Article Structure

The BOL has 18 articles — not 13, not 16, not “around 15.” Getting the article count right is the single most basic accuracy test for any BARMM legal document. The following table is your essential reference:

Art. Subject
I Name and Purpose
II Bangsamoro Identity
III Territorial Jurisdiction
IV General Principles and Policies
V Powers of Government (55 enumerated in Sec. 2)
VI Intergovernmental Relations
VII Bangsamoro Government (Parliament, Chief Minister, Cabinet)
VIII Wali (Ceremonial Head)
IX Basic Rights (incl. IP rights Sec. 3, Settler communities Sec. 15)
X Justice System (Shari’ah courts)
XI National Defense and Security
XII Fiscal Autonomy (Block Grant Secs. 15-16)
XIII Regional Economy and Patrimony
XIV Rehabilitation and Development (SDF Sec. 2)
XV Plebiscite
XVI Transition (BTA mandate, timeline, priority codes)
XVII Amendments
XVIII Final Provisions

Memorize this table. Better yet, print it and keep it within arm’s reach whenever you work on BARMM legal research. Every BOL citation you write should be checked against this map.

5.1.2 The Three Power Clauses: Residual, Enumerated, and General Welfare

Chapter 3 introduced the three relationships to national law (enumerated powers with no consistency requirement, powers requiring national law consistency, residual national powers). This section focuses on the three BOL provisions that define the scope of Bangsamoro authority. You must be able to find, quote, and apply all three.

The Residual Clause — Article V, Section 1:

All powers, functions, and responsibilities not granted by the Constitution or by national law to the Bangsamoro Government shall be vested in the National Government.6

This is the boundary line. If a power is not granted to the Bangsamoro Government by the Constitution (through Article X, Section 20) or by national law (through the BOL), it remains with the National Government. The practical effect: when you search the BOL for authority over a subject and find nothing, your answer is not “the BOL is silent.” Your answer is “the power is with the National Government under the residual clause.”

The 55 Enumerated Powers — Article V, Section 2:

Subject to Section 20, Article X of the Constitution and this Organic Law, the Bangsamoro Government shall exercise its authority over the following matters without prejudice to the general supervision of the President of the Republic of the Philippines:7

This section lists 55 areas from (a) “Administration of justice” through (ccc) “Water supply and services, flood control, and irrigation systems.” Every one of the 55 enumerated powers is a subject on which the Parliament can enact a BAA. The list is your primary tool for answering the question “Does the Bangsamoro Government have authority over this subject?”

Here is the complete enumeration, grouped by broad subject for research convenience:

Governance and Administration - (a) Administration of justice - (b) Administrative organization - (i) Civil service - (l) Creation, division, merger, abolition or alteration of boundaries of municipalities and barangays - (m) Creation of government-owned or controlled corporations and pioneer firms - (n) Creation of sources of revenues

Economic Development - (c) Agriculture, livestock, and food security - (e) Barter trade and countertrade - (g) Business name registration - (k) Cooperatives and social entrepreneurship - (o) Cultural exchange, and economic and technical cooperation - (u) Economic zones, industrial centers, and free ports - (ii) Islamic banking and finance - (oo) Power sector investments - (pp) Public utilities’ operations - (yy) Tourism development - (zz) Trade and industry

Land, Natural Resources, and Environment - (d) Ancestral domain and natural resources - (h) Cadastral land survey - (j) Classification of public lands - (t) Ecological solid waste management and pollution control - (x) Environment, parks, forest management, wildlife, and nature reserves conservation - (y) Fishery, marine, and aquatic resources - (gg) Inland waters - (hh) Inland waterways for navigation - (aaa) Urban and rural planning development - (bbb) Urban land reform and land use

Social Services and Human Development - (r) Development programs and laws for women, labor, the youth, the elderly, the differently-abled, and indigenous peoples - (s) Disaster risk reduction and management - (v) Education and skills training - (aa) Hajj and Umrah - (bb) Health - (cc) Housing and human settlements - (dd) Humanitarian services and institutions - (ee) Human rights - (ff) Indigenous peoples’ rights - (jj) Labor, employment, and occupation - (mm) Mechanisms for consultations for women and marginalized sectors - (nn) People’s organizations - (ss) Registration of births, marriages, and deaths - (tt) Regulation of the manufacture and distribution of food, drinks, drugs, and tobacco - (uu) Science and technology, research councils and scholarship programs - (vv) Social services, social welfare, and charities - (ww) Sports and recreation - (xx) Technical cooperation for human resource development

Infrastructure and Utilities - (qq) Public works and infrastructure - (ccc) Water supply and services, flood control, and irrigation systems

Finance and Revenue - (f) Budgeting - (ll) Loans, credits, and other forms of indebtedness - (z) Grants and donations

Culture, Law, and Identity - (p) Culture and language - (kk) Libraries and museums, and historical, cultural, and archaeological sites - (q) Customary laws - (rr) Quarantine regulations

The General Welfare Clause — Article V, Section 3:

The Bangsamoro Government shall exercise the powers expressly granted, those necessarily implied therefrom, as well as powers necessary, appropriate, or incidental for its efficient and effective governance and those which are essential to the promotion of general welfare.8

This clause extends authority beyond the literal 55 enumerated powers in two ways:

  1. Implied powers — powers necessarily implied from an express grant. Example: the power over “education and skills training” (v) necessarily implies the power to certify educational institutions and accredit teachers operating within BARMM.

  2. Incidental powers — powers necessary, appropriate, or incidental for efficient governance and the promotion of general welfare. Example: the power over “health” (bb) combined with the general welfare clause supports the creation of a regional health insurance scheme, even though “health insurance” is not explicitly listed.

The general welfare clause is powerful but bounded. It cannot create authority where no express power supports it. If none of the 55 enumerated powers relates to a subject — say, national defense — the general welfare clause cannot fill that gap. Article V, Section 1 (the residual clause) sends that power to the National Government.

5.1.3 Article-by-Article Research Guide

This section provides a research-oriented summary of each BOL article. Use it when you need to quickly locate which article addresses a particular subject.

Article I — Name and Purpose (Secs. 1-3)

Establishes the political entity’s name (“Bangsamoro Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao”) and its purpose: “meaningful self-governance within the framework of the Constitution.” Cite Article I, Section 3 when you need the purpose clause as a foundation for interpreting other BOL provisions.

Article II — Bangsamoro Identity (Secs. 1-2)

Defines the Bangsamoro people (“those who, at the advent of the Spanish colonization, were considered natives or original inhabitants of Mindanao and the Sulu archipelago”) and authorizes the Parliament to adopt the official flag, emblem, and hymn. Section 1 is essential for research involving the definition of who qualifies as Bangsamoro.

Article III — Territorial Jurisdiction (Secs. 1-5)

Defines the geographic scope of the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region — the areas included, the municipal and regional waters (up to 15 and 19 kilometers respectively), and the constituent units. Cite this article when your research involves questions about geographic jurisdiction — where Bangsamoro laws apply and where they do not.

Article IV — General Principles and Policies (Secs. 1-10)

Contains foundational policy declarations: territorial integrity (Sec. 1), self-governance (Sec. 2), parliamentary form of government (Sec. 3), social justice (Sec. 7), IP rights (Sec. 9), freedom of choice (Sec. 10). These are interpretive guides — they shape how courts and officials read the rest of the BOL.

Article V — Powers of Government (Secs. 1-3)

The heart of the BOL for legal researchers. Section 1 (residual clause), Section 2 (55 enumerated powers), Section 3 (general welfare clause). Almost every research question about BARMM authority starts here.

Article VI — Intergovernmental Relations (Secs. 1-13)

Creates the intergovernmental mechanisms: the Intergovernmental Relations Body (Sec. 2), the Philippine Congress-Bangsamoro Parliament Forum (Sec. 3), the Intergovernmental Fiscal Policy Board (Sec. 4), and the Council of Leaders (Sec. 9). Also covers national programs that continue in BARMM (Sec. 13). Cite this article when your research involves coordination between BARMM and the National Government.

Article VII — Bangsamoro Government (Secs. 1-42)

The longest article. Covers: the Parliament’s powers (Sec. 5), composition (Secs. 6-10), legislative procedures (Sec. 25), the appropriations power (Sec. 27), the Chief Minister’s election and powers (Secs. 30-34), the Deputy Chief Ministers (Sec. 35), vote of no confidence (Sec. 36), civil service (Sec. 39), and accountability (Sec. 41). This article is the source for anything about how the Bangsamoro Government is structured and how it operates.

Article VIII — Wali (Secs. 1-6)

Covers the Wali — the ceremonial head. Appointment (Sec. 1), qualifications (Sec. 2), duties (Sec. 3), term (Sec. 4), per diem (Sec. 5), removal (Sec. 6). Short and narrowly scoped. Do not confuse this article with Article XVI (Transition).

Article IX — Basic Rights (Secs. 1-26)

A broad article covering: transitional justice (Sec. 1), IP rights (Sec. 3), religious freedom (Sec. 5), human rights (Sec. 6), social justice and access to basic services (Sec. 8), labor rights (Sec. 10), women’s participation (Sec. 11), settler communities (Sec. 15), education (Secs. 16-19), science and technology (Sec. 21), health (Sec. 22), arts and culture (Secs. 24-26). Do not confuse this article with Article X (Justice System).

Article X — Justice System (Secs. 1-20)

Covers the Bangsamoro justice system: Shari’ah courts (Sec. 2), their jurisdiction (Secs. 5-7), the Shari’ah High Court (Sec. 7), judicial qualifications (Sec. 8), traditional/tribal justice (Sec. 17), and alternative dispute resolution (Sec. 20). Cite this article for questions about the court system, Shari’ah jurisdiction, and dispute resolution.

Article XI — National Defense and Security (Secs. 1-7)

Short and clear: national defense is a National Government responsibility (Sec. 1). The PNP creates a Police Regional Office in BARMM (Sec. 2). The BJMP and BFP establish regional offices (Secs. 5-6). The Philippine Coast Guard establishes a regional office (Sec. 7). This article is relevant when researching the boundary between BARMM authority and National Government security powers.

Article XII — Fiscal Autonomy (Secs. 1-41)

The most section-heavy article, covering: revenue sources (Sec. 6), taxing power limitations (Sec. 9), tax sharing (Sec. 10), the block grant (Secs. 15-16), automatic appropriation (Sec. 17), block grant allocation rules (Sec. 19), general spending limitations (Sec. 20), natural resource revenue sharing (Sec. 34), and the Intergovernmental Fiscal Policy Board’s functions (Secs. 37-38). Cite this article for all fiscal, budget, and revenue questions. Block grant provisions are in Sections 15-16 — not Section 9.

Article XIII — Regional Economy and Patrimony (Secs. 1-36)

Covers: sustainable development (Secs. 2-3), the BEDC (Sec. 4), gender and development (Sec. 5), natural resources (Secs. 8-16), the Zones of Joint Cooperation (Secs. 18-21), inland waters management (Sec. 22), agriculture and fisheries (Sec. 23), trade and industry (Secs. 24-28), halal program (Sec. 30), energy (Secs. 31-36). Cite this article for research involving economic development, natural resources, and patrimony.

Article XIV — Rehabilitation and Development (Secs. 1-2)

Short. Establishes the Special Development Fund (Sec. 2) for rehabilitation, reconstruction, and development. Cite this article for research involving post-conflict development and the SDF.

Article XV — Plebiscite (Secs. 1-3)

Governs the plebiscite for ratification of the BOL. Mostly historical relevance now, but cite it when tracing the legal basis for the BOL’s effectivity.

Article XVI — Transition (Secs. 1-14)

Covers the BTA: creation (Sec. 2), powers during transition (Sec. 3), priority legislation (Sec. 4 — the five priority codes: Administrative Code, Revenue Code, Electoral Code, Local Government Code, Education Code), the transition plan (Sec. 6), the interim cabinet of 15 ministries (Sec. 8), disposition of ARMM personnel (Sec. 10), dissolution upon the first Parliament’s election (Sec. 12). Do not confuse with Article VIII (Wali).

Article XVII — Amendments (Sec. 1)

One section: amendments, revisions, or repeals of the BOL must be enacted by Congress. The Parliament cannot amend the BOL on its own.

Article XVIII — Final Provisions (Secs. 1-5)

Covers: subsisting regional laws (Sec. 1), existing LGUs (Sec. 2), separability clause (Sec. 3), amendatory clause (Sec. 4), and effectivity (Sec. 5). Cite this article when researching the continued validity of pre-BOL ARMM laws or the BOL’s relationship with other national laws.

5.2 Researching BOL Authority: Explicit vs. Implied

When a research question asks “Does the Bangsamoro Government have authority over X?” you must conduct a two-stage analysis: first check for explicit authority, then assess implied authority.

5.2.1 Explicit Authority Analysis

Step 1: Search Article V, Section 2. Scan the 55 enumerated powers for a direct match. Use the grouped list in Section 5.1.3 of this chapter to narrow your search by subject category.

Step 2: Search the subject-specific article. The 55 enumerated powers in Article V are broad labels. The detail is in the subject-specific articles. For example, the enumerated power over “education and skills training” (Art. V, Sec. 2(v)) is just a label. The substance — what the Bangsamoro Government can actually do with that power — is in Article IX, Sections 16-19, which cover the integrated education system, Madaris education, the tribal university system, and sports development.

Step 3: Check for conditions and limitations. Many BOL provisions grant authority with conditions attached. Article VII, Section 39 grants authority over civil service — but “in case of conflict with the national laws, the Constitution and existing national civil service laws, rules, and regulations shall prevail.”9 Article XII, Section 6 grants taxing power — but Section 9 lists 12 categories of taxes the Bangsamoro Government cannot impose.10 Record both the grant and its conditions.

Step 4: Check Article XVI, Section 4 for BTA-specific mandates. If you are researching during the transition period, Article XVI, Section 4 identifies priority legislation: the Bangsamoro Administrative Code, Revenue Code, Electoral Code, Local Government Code, and Education Code.11 These are subjects the BOL explicitly delegated to the BTA for further legislation.

5.2.2 Implied Authority Analysis

If explicit authority does not resolve your question, assess whether the general welfare clause (Art. V, Sec. 3) supports the proposed action.

Apply this three-part test:

  1. Is there an express power that relates to the subject? Implied authority must be traceable to an express grant. If no enumerated power touches the subject, the general welfare clause cannot create authority from nothing.

  2. Is the proposed action “necessarily implied” from the express power? If yes, it is an implied power. Example: the express power over “health” (bb) necessarily implies the power to license hospitals within BARMM.

  3. Is the proposed action “necessary, appropriate, or incidental” for efficient governance? If yes, it is an incidental power under the general welfare clause. This is broader than “necessarily implied” but still requires a connection to an express power.

If the answer to all three questions is “no,” the power does not exist for the Bangsamoro Government. The residual clause (Art. V, Sec. 1) assigns it to the National Government.

5.3 Tracing BOL Provisions to Their Constitutional Basis

Every BOL power has a constitutional source. Tracing that source strengthens your research because it (a) confirms that the BOL provision is constitutionally valid, (b) reveals the constitutional limits that shape the BOL provision, and (c) provides a stronger legal foundation for any document you produce.

5.3.1 The Tracing Method

Follow this three-step sequence:

Step 1: Identify the BOL provision. Start with the BOL article and section you are researching.

Step 2: Find the constitutional authorization. Ask: “Which constitutional provision authorizes or relates to this BOL provision?” Use the six-article map from Section 4.2.2 as your guide.

Common traces:

BOL Provision Constitutional Basis
Art. V, Sec. 2 (55 enumerated powers) Art. X, Sec. 20 (minimum powers for organic acts)
Art. VII, Sec. 2 (Parliament’s legislative authority) Art. X, Secs. 1, 15, 18 (creation, organic act, self-governance)
Art. VII, Sec. 27 (appropriations requirement) Art. VI, Sec. 29 (no money from Treasury without appropriation)
Art. VII, Sec. 41 (public office as public trust) Art. XI, Sec. 1 (accountability of public officers)
Art. IX, Sec. 3 (IP rights) Art. X, Sec. 20 + Art. XIII (social justice)
Art. IX, Sec. 16 (education) Art. XIV, Secs. 1-5 (education provisions)
Art. XII, Secs. 15-16 (block grant) Art. X, Secs. 17, 20 (tax sharing, minimum powers)
Art. XII, Sec. 2 (COA as exclusive auditor) Art. IX-D (COA’s constitutional mandate)

Step 3: Note the constitutional constraint. The constitutional provision does not just authorize — it also limits. Article X, Section 20 lists nine minimum power areas but also states the organic act must be “within the framework of this Constitution.” Article XIV guarantees academic freedom — so a BAA on education cannot restrict what universities teach. Record both the authorization and the constraint.

5.3.2 When the Trace Reveals a Gap

Sometimes you will find a BOL provision that does not trace neatly to a specific constitutional provision. This does not automatically mean the provision is unconstitutional. Article X, Section 20(9) includes a catch-all: “such other matters as may be authorized by law for the promotion of the general welfare within the region.”12 This is the constitutional hook for BOL powers that go beyond the eight specific areas listed in Section 20.

However, if the BOL provision conflicts with a specific constitutional prohibition — such as the Bill of Rights or the fiscal provisions of Article VI — the constitutional prohibition controls regardless of the catch-all.

5.4 Identifying BOL Delegations to Parliament

The BOL does not resolve every legal question. In many areas, it deliberately delegates subjects to Parliament for further legislation. Recognizing these delegations is critical because they tell you two things: (1) Parliament has authority to legislate on the subject, and (2) the BOL’s provisions on the subject are not self-executing — they require implementing legislation.

5.4.1 How to Spot a Delegation

Delegation clauses use characteristic language. Look for these phrases:

  • “The Parliament shall enact a law…” — direct delegation.
  • “…as may be provided by law” — signals that a future BAA must provide the details.
  • “…as the Parliament may provide” — same effect.
  • “…shall be defined by the Parliament” — delegation of definitional authority.
  • “…in accordance with a law to be passed by the Parliament” — condition precedent.

5.4.2 Key Delegations in the BOL

The BOL contains dozens of delegations. Here are the most significant:

BOL Provision Subject Delegated Status
Art. XVI, Sec. 4(a) Bangsamoro Administrative Code Enacted (BAA 13)
Art. XVI, Sec. 4(a) Bangsamoro Revenue Code Not yet enacted
Art. XVI, Sec. 4(a) Bangsamoro Electoral Code Enacted (BAA 35)
Art. XVI, Sec. 4(a) Bangsamoro Local Governance Code Enacted (BAA 49)
Art. XVI, Sec. 4(a) Bangsamoro Education Code Enacted (BAA 18)
Art. VII, Sec. 39 Bangsamoro Civil Service Code Enacted (BAA 17)
Art. IX, Sec. 1 Transitional justice mechanism Enacted (BAA 89)
Art. IX, Sec. 9 Housing and human settlements agency Enacted through BAA 13 provisions
Art. X, Sec. 4 Shari’ah laws (personal, family, property) Pending
Art. X, Sec. 17 Traditional/tribal justice systems law Pending
Art. XIII, Sec. 22 Inland waters management law Pending

When your research identifies a delegation that has been fulfilled by a BAA, your next step is to read that BAA (Tiers 3-4, covered in Chapters 5-6). When the delegation remains unfulfilled, you have identified a legislative gap — the BOL authorizes Parliament to act, but no BAA yet exists on the subject.

5.5 Shari’ah Checkpoint for Tiers 1 and 2

At the end of every tier-level research session, apply the Shari’ah Checkpoint. For Tiers 1 and 2, answer these three questions:

Question 1: Does the constitutional provision invoke or affect religious freedom?

Article III, Section 5 of the Constitution guarantees free exercise of religion. Article XIV, Section 3(3) allows religious instruction in public schools at the option of parents or guardians. If your research touches religious practice, religious education, or religious institutions, flag these provisions.

Question 2: Does the BOL provision relate to a subject that intersects with Shari’ah?

The BOL explicitly recognizes Shari’ah in multiple articles. Article X establishes the Shari’ah court system and grants Parliament power over Shari’ah law on personal, family, and property matters (Sec. 4). Article IV, Section 3 establishes the parliamentary form of government. Article IX, Section 5 guarantees religious freedom within the Bangsamoro context. If your research question falls within any of these areas, note the Shari’ah dimension for deeper analysis in later tiers (the Shari’ah analysis framework is covered in Chapter 9).

Question 3: Does the subject involve customary law provisions?

The BOL separately recognizes customary law alongside Shari’ah. Article IV, Section 9 requires the Bangsamoro Government to recognize IP rights. Article V, Section 2(q) enumerates “customary laws” as a Bangsamoro power. Article X, Section 17 calls for laws to promote traditional/tribal justice systems. If your research question involves indigenous communities, land, or disputes, both Shari’ah and customary law dimensions may be present.

Record your Shari’ah Checkpoint findings in your Research Memorandum (Chapter 12 format).

5.6 Worked Example: Education Authority

This section traces the Bangsamoro Government’s authority over education from the Constitution through the BOL to enacted legislation.

The Research Question

“What is the legal basis for the Bangsamoro Government’s authority over basic education within its territory?”

Step 1: Constitutional Basis (Tier 1)

Search Article XIV (Education, Science, Technology, Arts, Culture, Sports).

Article XIV, Section 1:

The State shall protect and promote the right of all citizens to quality education at all levels, and shall take appropriate steps to make such education accessible to all.13

This establishes the constitutional duty. It applies to the Bangsamoro Government as part of the Philippine state.

Article XIV, Section 2(1):

The State shall establish, maintain, and support a complete, adequate, and integrated system of education relevant to the needs of the people and society.

Article XIV, Section 3(2):

Educational institutions, other than those established by religious groups and mission boards, shall be owned solely by citizens of the Philippines or corporations or associations at least sixty per centum of the capital of which is owned by such citizens.

Article X, Section 20(7): The organic act must grant the autonomous region power over “educational policies.” This is the constitutional delegation that empowers the BOL to assign education authority to BARMM.

Step 2: BOL Provisions (Tier 2)

Article V, Section 2(v): “Education and skills training” is one of the 55 enumerated powers. This is the express grant.

Article IX, Sections 16-19 provide the substance:

  • Section 16 (Integrated System of Quality Education): “It shall be a top priority of the Bangsamoro Government to establish, maintain, and support a complete and integrated system of quality education, which shall be a subsystem of the national education system.”14 Note the phrase “subsystem of the national education system” — it means BARMM’s education system operates within, not outside, the national framework. The section also requires conformity with “minimum standards set by the National Government.”

  • Section 17 (Vocational, Technical, Nonformal, and Special Education): Extends coverage to vocational and nonformal education, again conforming to national minimum standards.

  • Section 18 (Madaris Educational System): Authorizes the Bangsamoro Government to “establish, maintain, and supervise Madaris education” in coordination with DepEd, CHED, and TESDA.15 This is a uniquely Bangsamoro power — no other region has explicit BOL authority over Madaris education.

  • Section 19 (Tribal University System): Directs Parliament to create a tribal university system for non-Moro indigenous peoples.

Article XVI, Section 4(a): Lists the “Bangsamoro Education Code” among the BTA’s priority legislation. This delegation has been fulfilled: Parliament enacted BAA 18 (Bangsamoro Education Code).

Step 3: Trace the Chain

Here is the complete authority chain:

Level Source Provision
Constitutional duty Art. XIV, Sec. 1 State shall protect and promote quality education
Constitutional delegation Art. X, Sec. 20(7) Organic act shall grant power over educational policies
BOL express power Art. V, Sec. 2(v) “Education and skills training” enumerated
BOL substantive provisions Art. IX, Secs. 16-19 Integrated education system, Madaris, tribal university
BOL delegation to Parliament Art. XVI, Sec. 4(a) Priority: enact Bangsamoro Education Code
Enacted legislation (Tier 4) BAA 18 Bangsamoro Education Code

This chain shows how constitutional authorization flows through the organic act to enacted legislation. The same method works for any subject within the 55 enumerated powers.

Shari’ah Checkpoint

Does the education research involve Shari’ah or customary law? Yes. Article IX, Section 18 covers Madaris education — the Islamic educational system. The Shari’ah dimension is present. Flag it for deeper treatment when researching Tiers 3-4 (specifically BAA 18’s provisions on Islamic and Arabic studies).

5.7 Common Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Citing the wrong BOL article number. Prevention: check every article citation against the 18-article table in Section 5.1.1. Treat this as a mandatory verification step, not a courtesy check.

Pitfall 2: Paraphrasing the BOL from memory instead of quoting verbatim. Prevention: always work with the BOL text open in front of you. Quote the actual language. If you cannot access the text, mark your citation as “[UNVERIFIED]” and verify before finalizing.

Pitfall 3: Assuming the general welfare clause creates unlimited authority. Prevention: the general welfare clause (Art. V, Sec. 3) extends existing powers — it does not create new ones. Always trace the implied or incidental power back to a specific enumerated power.

Pitfall 4: Treating all constitutional provisions as self-executing. Prevention: distinguish between self-executing provisions (Bill of Rights) and directive principles (social justice aspirations). Only self-executing provisions override BAAs directly. Directive principles require implementing legislation.

Pitfall 5: Ignoring the conditions attached to BOL grants of authority. Prevention: when you find a power grant, read the full section. Look for provisos, conditions, and cross-references to other articles. Many grants come with conditions (consistency with national law, compliance with minimum standards, COA oversight).

Pitfall 6: Skipping Article XVI when researching BTA-era questions. Prevention: if your research involves the transition period, start with Article XVI before looking at Articles V and VII. The BTA’s powers are defined through Article XVI’s lens.

Pitfall 7: Citing Art. XII, Sec. 9 for the Block Grant. Prevention: Section 9 covers taxing power limitations. The Block Grant is in Sections 15-16. Memorize this distinction — it appears in BARMM documents frequently, and it is wrong every time.

Template 5B: BOL Article Quick Reference Map

Post this table at your workstation. Use it to verify every BOL citation before you finalize any document.

Art. Subject Key Sections
I Name and Purpose Sec. 3 (purpose clause)
II Bangsamoro Identity Sec. 1 (definition), Sec. 2 (symbols)
III Territorial Jurisdiction Sec. 2 (composition), Sec. 4 (waters)
IV General Principles and Policies Sec. 3 (parliamentary government), Sec. 7 (social justice)
V Powers of Government Sec. 1 (residual), Sec. 2 (55 enumerated), Sec. 3 (general welfare)
VI Intergovernmental Relations Sec. 2 (IRB), Sec. 4 (IGFPB), Sec. 9 (Council of Leaders)
VII Bangsamoro Government Sec. 2 (powers), Sec. 5 (Parliament powers), Sec. 25 (procedures), Sec. 27 (appropriations), Sec. 32 (CM powers)
VIII Wali Sec. 1 (appointment), Sec. 3 (duties)
IX Basic Rights Sec. 3 (IP rights), Sec. 15 (settlers), Secs. 16-19 (education), Sec. 22 (health)
X Justice System Secs. 5-7 (Shari’ah court jurisdiction), Sec. 17 (tribal justice)
XI National Defense and Security Sec. 1 (national responsibility), Sec. 2 (PNP regional office)
XII Fiscal Autonomy Sec. 6 (revenue sources), Sec. 9 (taxing limits), Secs. 15-16 (block grant), Sec. 34 (natural resources)
XIII Regional Economy and Patrimony Sec. 4 (BEDC), Sec. 22 (inland waters), Sec. 28 (economic zones)
XIV Rehabilitation and Development Sec. 2 (Special Development Fund)
XV Plebiscite Secs. 1-3 (ratification)
XVI Transition Sec. 2 (BTA creation), Sec. 4 (priority codes), Sec. 8 (15 ministries), Sec. 12 (dissolution)
XVII Amendments Sec. 1 (Congress only)
XVIII Final Provisions Sec. 1 (subsisting laws), Sec. 4 (repealing clause)

5.9 Quality Checklist

Before concluding your Tier 1-2 research, verify all of the following:

  • ☐ Constitutional provisions identified. You have found all constitutional articles relevant to your research question, not just Article X.
  • ☐ Constitutional provisions classified. Each is marked as self-executing or directive.
  • ☐ BOL article numbers verified. Every BOL citation has been checked against the 18-article map (Template 5B).
  • ☐ Dangerous swaps checked. You have specifically verified that you have not confused Art. IX/X, Art. V/VI, Art. VII/VIII, Art. VIII/XVI, or Art. XII Sec. 9/Secs. 15-16.
  • ☐ Express authority analyzed. You have searched Article V, Section 2 for the relevant enumerated power and read the corresponding subject-specific article.
  • ☐ Implied authority assessed. If express authority is insufficient, you have applied the three-part test from Section 5.2.2.
  • ☐ Conditions and limitations recorded. You have noted every proviso, consistency requirement, and national law reference attached to the BOL provisions you found.
  • ☐ Constitutional basis traced. Each BOL provision in your research is linked to its constitutional authorization.
  • ☐ Delegations identified. You have noted whether the BOL delegates the subject to Parliament and whether that delegation has been fulfilled.
  • ☐ Shari’ah Checkpoint completed. You have answered the three Shari’ah Checkpoint questions from Section 4.7.
  • ☐ All quotations are verbatim. No paraphrased BOL or constitutional text appears as a direct quote.
  • ☐ Constitutional Provision Tracker completed (Template 5A) with all relevant provisions.

06
BAA 13 and Charter BAAs: The Institutional Framework

Chapter 6 — BAA 13 and Charter BAAs: The Institutional Framework

Chapters 4 and 5 covered the Constitution and the Bangsamoro Organic Law. This chapter takes you into the heart of Bangsamoro legislation: Tier 3 (BAA 13, the Bangsamoro Administrative Code) and the Charter BAAs — the comprehensive code BAAs that establish complete institutional frameworks for entire sectors.

BAA 13 is the government’s organizational charter. The charter BAAs — BAA 17 (Civil Service Code), BAA 18 (Education Code), BAA 35 (Electoral Code), BAA 49 (Local Governance Code), BAA 82 (Labor Code), BAA 84 (Budget System Act) — are the foundational laws that operationalize BOL powers through institutional structures. Together, they define the operational architecture of the Bangsamoro Government.

Chapter 7 covers other Bangsamoro Autonomy Acts (sector-specific, appropriations, municipalities, symbols).

6.1 Tier 3: BAA 13 — The Bangsamoro Administrative Code

6.1.1 What BAA 13 Is

BAA 13, formally titled “An Act Providing for the Bangsamoro Administrative Code and for Other Related Purposes,” is the structural law of the Bangsamoro Government. Its purpose, stated in Introductory Provisions Section 2, is “to prescribe the structural, functional and procedural principles and rules of governance of the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao.”[^1]

Think of BAA 13 as the government’s organizational charter. It does four things:

  1. Defines the territory, symbols, and foundational principles of the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region (Book I)
  2. Establishes intergovernmental relations between BARMM, the National Government, and local government units (Book II)
  3. Creates the government structure — the Parliament, the Wali, the Chief Minister, and the Cabinet (Books III, IV, V)
  4. Organizes every ministry and office — specifying mandates, powers and functions, and organizational structures (Book VI)

BAA 13 is not just another BAA. It is the creating law for most ministries, offices, and agencies in the Bangsamoro Government. When a minister needs to know the legal basis for their ministry’s existence, they start with BAA 13. When a researcher needs to determine who has authority over a specific government function, they start with BAA 13.

6.1.2 The Seven-Book Structure

BAA 13 is organized into seven Books. Each Book addresses a distinct dimension of governance:

Book Title Content Key Use for Researchers
I Bangsamoro Autonomy and Administration Territory, symbols, legal principles, public officers, immunity from suit, oaths, reports Foundational rules — definitions, effectivity of laws, the Bangsamoro Gazette, legal periods
II Intergovernmental Relations IGRB, Council of Leaders, IGR principles, national-regional coordination Authority boundaries between BARMM and the National Government
III The Bangsamoro Autonomous Government Powers of government, the Wali, the Parliament, the Chief Minister, the judiciary Separation of powers — who does what at the highest level
IV The Office of the Chief Minister Chief Minister powers, OCM organization, executive offices (BAGO, Senior Minister, Cabinet Secretary), BMSS Executive authority, appointment power, executive issuances, OCM-attached offices
V General Functions of the Cabinet Cabinet Ministers’ collective functions Cabinet-level decision-making authority
VI Ministries and Offices All 15 ministries plus commissions and bodies — each with Declaration of Policy, Mandate, Powers and Functions, Organizational Structure The most-used Book — where you find institutional mandates and specific powers
VII General Provisions Applicability, effectivity, transitory provisions Scope and effective date

Book VI is your primary research destination. It contains 20 Titles covering every ministry, commission, and body in the Bangsamoro Government. Each Title follows a consistent internal structure:

  • Chapter 1: General Provisions — Declaration of Policy, Mandate, Powers and Functions
  • Chapter 2: Ministry Proper — Office of the Minister, powers of the Minister
  • Additional Chapters — Bureaus, offices, attached agencies, organizational details

This consistent structure means once you learn how to read one ministry’s section, you can navigate all of them.

6.1.3 The Mandate and Powers/Functions Distinction

Every ministry section in Book VI contains two critical provisions that researchers must distinguish:

The Mandate defines what the ministry exists to do — its reason for being, stated in broad terms.

The Powers and Functions define what the ministry is authorized to do — the specific actions it can take to carry out its mandate.

These are not the same thing. The mandate is the mission statement. The powers and functions are the legal authorities. A ministry can have a broad mandate but limited enumerated powers. When your research question asks “Does Ministry X have authority to do Y?”, look at the Powers and Functions section, not just the mandate.

Example: Ministry of Environment, Natural Resources and Energy (MENRE)

The MENRE mandate (Book VI, Title III, Section 2) states:

“The Ministry shall be primarily responsible for the exploration, utilization, management, conservation, protection and sustainable development of the region’s environment, natural resources and energy sources.”[^2]

The powers and functions (Section 3) then list 19 specific authorities, from recommending regional environmental laws (item a) to promulgating rules for controlling pollution (item m) to performing “such other acts as may be necessary, proper or incidental to the attainment of its mandates” (item s).[^3]

If someone asks, “Can MENRE regulate water pollution in Lake Lanao?”, the answer comes from the powers and functions — specifically items (c) and (m), which authorize promulgating rules governing “activities tending to cause the depletion and degradation of our environment” and “the control of water, air and land pollution within the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region.” The mandate alone is too general to give a definitive answer.

6.1.4 When BAA 13 Creates vs. When a Standalone BAA Creates

BAA 13 is the creating law for most government entities. But not all of them. Some entities were created by standalone BAAs before BAA 13 was enacted, and BAA 13 later incorporated references to them. Others were created by standalone BAAs after BAA 13 because the entity required more detail than a section within an administrative code could provide.

Here is how to tell the difference:

Entity Creating Law BAA 13 Role
Ministry of Agriculture, Fisheries and Agrarian Reform (MAFAR) BAA 13, Book VI, Title I Creating law — mandate and powers defined here
Ministry of Finance, Budget and Management (MFBM) BAA 13, Book VI, Title IV Creating law
All 15 Ministries BAA 13, Book VI, Titles I-XV Creating law for each
Bangsamoro Human Rights Commission (BHRC) BAA 4 BAA 13 references BAA 4 in Book VI, Title XVII, Chapter 1, Sec. 2
Bangsamoro Attorney-General’s Office (BAGO) BAA 5 BAA 13 provides detail in Book IV, Title II, Sec. 10-24, but references BAA 5 as the creating law
Bangsamoro Women Commission (BWC) BAA 8 BAA 13 provides detail in Book VI, Title XVI
Bangsamoro Youth Commission (BYC) BAA 10 BAA 13 references BYC in Book VI, Title XVII
Bangsamoro Sports Commission (BSC) BAA 12 BAA 13 references BSC in Book VI, Title XVII
Bangsamoro Sustainable Development Board (BSDB) BAA 31 Created after BAA 13; not in the original administrative code
Bangsamoro Nutrition Commission BAA 83 Created after BAA 13
Bangsamoro Transitional Justice Commission BAA 89 Created after BAA 13

The research rule: Always check both BAA 13 and any standalone creating BAA. BAA 13 may provide the organizational structure while the standalone BAA provides additional powers, funding provisions, or operational details not found in the administrative code. When the two conflict, apply the lex specialis rule (Section 6.2.5): the specific standalone BAA prevails over the general administrative code for matters it specifically addresses.

6.1.5 How to Search BAA 13 Systematically

BAA 13 is a large document — over 5,000 lines of text covering the entire government structure. You cannot skim it and expect to find what you need. Use this systematic approach:

Step 1: Identify the relevant Book.

Your Question Concerns… Start With…
Territory, symbols, legal definitions, public officers Book I
BARMM-National Government relations, IGR bodies Book II
Parliament powers, Wali, Chief Minister, judiciary Book III
Chief Minister powers, OCM offices, executive issuances Book IV
Cabinet functions Book V
A specific ministry’s mandate, powers, or structure Book VI
General applicability and effectivity Book VII

Step 2: Identify the relevant Title within the Book.

For Book VI (your most common destination), the 20 Titles correspond to specific ministries and bodies:

Title Ministry/Body
I Agriculture, Fisheries and Agrarian Reform (MAFAR)
II Basic, Higher, and Technical Education (MBHTE)
III Environment, Natural Resources and Energy (MENRE)
IV Finance, Budget and Management (MFBM)
V Health (MOH)
VI Human Settlements and Development (MHSD)
VII Indigenous Peoples’ Affairs (MIPA)
VIII Interior and Local Government (MILG)
IX Labor and Employment (MOLE)
X Public Order and Safety (MPOS)
XI Public Works (MPW)
XII Science and Technology (MOST)
XIII Social Services and Development (MSSD)
XIV Trade, Investments, and Tourism (MTIT)
XV Transportation and Communications (MOTC)
XVI Bangsamoro Commissions (BWC and others)
XVII Other Commissions and Bodies (BHRC, BYC, BSC)
XVIII Administrative Services
XIX National Government Agencies in the BARMM
XX Government-Owned or -Controlled Corporations

Step 3: Within the Title, locate the specific provision.

For ministry research, follow this order:

  1. Section 1 (Declaration of Policy) — Read first for context. What policy goals does the ministry serve?
  2. Section 2 (Mandate) — The ministry’s mission. What is it created to do?
  3. Section 3 (Powers and Functions) — The specific legal authorities. This is where most research answers live.
  4. Organizational Structure sections — Who within the ministry handles the function you are researching?

Step 4: Cross-reference with other Books.

Your research rarely ends within a single Title. After finding the ministry-specific provisions:

  • Check Book IV if the function involves the Chief Minister’s power (appointment, executive orders, eminent domain)
  • Check Book II if the function involves coordination with the National Government
  • Check Book III if the function involves Parliament’s legislative authority or the judicial system
  • Check Book I for definitions, legal principles, and procedural rules that apply across all ministries

6.1.6 BAA 13 Amendments

BAA 13 has been amended once: BAA 37 changed Book I, Chapter 2, Section 9 to establish the seat of government in Parang, Maguindanao del Norte (the original provision placed it in Cotabato City “unless otherwise provided by the Bangsamoro Parliament in a subsequent law”).[^4]

Always check whether a provision you cite from BAA 13 has been amended. As of February 2026, BAA 37 is the only amendment — but more may follow as the Bangsamoro Government matures and the administrative code evolves.

6.2 Tier 4: Other Bangsamoro Autonomy Acts

6.2.1 The BAA Archive as a Searchable Corpus

As of February 2026, the Bangsamoro Parliament has enacted 89 BAAs.[^5] These form the Bangsamoro legislative archive — the body of enacted law that governs everything from the official flag (BAA 1) to transitional justice and reconciliation (BAA 89).

Five BAA numbers — 56, 66, 67, 68, and 69 — have no titles on public record as of this writing. The remaining 84 BAAs with known titles cover 13 domain categories. Understanding these categories is your first step toward efficient research.

6.2.2 Categories of BAAs

Not all BAAs serve the same legislative purpose. Sort them into six functional categories to guide your research:

Category 1: Creating/Charter BAAs

These BAAs establish new government entities — commissions, boards, offices, and institutions not created by BAA 13. Each creating BAA typically defines the entity’s mandate, powers and functions, composition, and funding.

BAA Entity Created
4 Bangsamoro Human Rights Commission (BHRC)
5 Bangsamoro Attorney-General’s Office (BAGO)
6 Bangsamoro Socio-Economic Development Planning System
8 Bangsamoro Women Commission (BWC)
10 Bangsamoro Youth Commission (BYC)
12 Bangsamoro Sports Commission (BSC)
31 Bangsamoro Sustainable Development Board (BSDB)
40 Bangsamoro Science High School System
50 Bangsamoro Kulliyyah for Islamic Studies
57 Office of the Bangsamoro Mujahideen
59 Bangsamoro Agriculture and Fisheries Training Institute
83 Bangsamoro Nutrition Commission
89 Bangsamoro Transitional Justice and Reconciliation Program

Research tip: When researching an entity’s authority, check both its creating BAA and its section in BAA 13. The creating BAA usually provides more detailed powers and operational provisions. BAA 13 provides the organizational framework and interrelationships.

Category 2: Comprehensive Code BAAs

These BAAs establish complete regulatory frameworks for entire sectors. They are the most substantive pieces of Bangsamoro legislation — often running dozens or hundreds of sections — and typically define rights, duties, penalties, and administrative procedures.

BAA Code
13 Bangsamoro Administrative Code
17 Bangsamoro Civil Service Code
18 Bangsamoro Education Code
35 Bangsamoro Electoral Code
49 Bangsamoro Local Governance Code
82 Bangsamoro Labor and Employment Code
84 Bangsamoro Budget System Act

These are the BAAs you will consult most often. Each represents a sector where Parliament has exercised comprehensive legislative authority. If your research question falls within one of these sectors, the relevant code is your primary source — not national law (unless the BOL requires consistency, as with civil service under Article VII, Section 39).

What has NOT been enacted: As of February 2026, the Bangsamoro Parliament has not enacted a Revenue Code, an Environment Code, a Procurement Code, a Health Code, or a Housing Code. These are legislative gaps — areas where the BOL grants power but Parliament has not yet exercised it comprehensively. Identifying these gaps is itself a research finding. When you discover that no comprehensive code exists for your research topic, document this as a gap and identify the applicable national laws that operate suppletorily in the absence of Bangsamoro legislation.

Other Bangsamoro Autonomy Acts

Chapter 7 — Other Bangsamoro Autonomy Acts

This chapter covers all Bangsamoro Autonomy Acts beyond BAA 13 and the charter BAAs discussed in Chapter 6. These BAAs cover electoral legislation, hospital infrastructure, municipality creation, sector-specific regulations, appropriations, and symbols and identity. Chapter 6 covered the institutional framework; this chapter covers how to research the broader legislative archive of 89 enacted BAAs.

Category 3: Sector-Specific BAAs

These BAAs address specific issues within a sector without creating a comprehensive code. They expand mandates, fill gaps, or address specific needs.

BAA Subject
9 Regulation of Recruitment Agencies
19 Overseas Bangsamoro Workers Protection
62 Internally Displaced Persons Protection
64 Indigenous Peoples Rights (strengthening MIPA)
72 Bereavement Leave (7-day forfeitable leave)
81 Salamat Excellence Award for Leadership Program

Research tip: Sector-specific BAAs often coexist with broader codes. BAA 72 (Bereavement Leave) operates alongside BAA 82 (Labor Code). BAA 64 (IP Rights) operates alongside BAA 13’s Title VII on Indigenous Peoples’ Affairs. Your research must capture both the specific BAA and the general framework.

These BAAs authorize government spending. They come in three sub-types:

Research relevance: Appropriations BAAs are not just budget documents. The General Provisions section of each GAA contains spending rules, restrictions, and conditions that operate as law for the fiscal year. When your research concerns government funding, procurement authority, or expenditure limits, check the latest GAA’s General Provisions alongside the Budget System Act (BAA 84).

If your research concerns jurisdiction over a specific geographic area, these BAAs may be relevant — particularly for recently created municipalities whose boundaries may overlap with previously existing ones.

Category 6: Symbols and Identity BAAs

These BAAs establish official symbols, holidays, and identity markers:

These are rarely the target of legal research, but they can be relevant for protocol questions, scheduling disputes, or regulatory compliance involving calendar-dependent deadlines.

7.1 Electoral BAAs: A Special Case

Electoral legislation in BARMM has its own complexity because it involves repeated amendment and even judicial invalidation. The electoral BAAs are:

BAA Subject Status
35 Bangsamoro Electoral Code Enacted; amended by BAA 87 and BAA 88
58 Parliamentary Districts Creation Struck down by Supreme Court (Oct 2025)
77 Reconstituted Parliamentary Districts (amends BAA 58) Struck down by Supreme Court (Oct 2025)
86 Parliamentary District Apportionment Enacted post-SC decision; establishes 32 districts
87 Removal of “None of the Above” option Amends BAA 35, Section 14, Article VII
88 Electoral Code Amendment Amends BAA 35

The parliamentary district saga — BAA 58 enacted, BAA 77 amending it, both struck down, BAA 86 replacing them — is the clearest example of why you must always verify whether a BAA is still good law. Section 7.3 covers this verification process.

7.2 Hospital and Health Infrastructure BAAs

A significant portion of BAAs — 13 out of 89 — concern hospital creation, upgrade, or conversion. These are:

BAA Subject Location
21 Oxygen tanks acquisition (COVID response) Region-wide

26 Wao District Hospital upgrade Lanao del Sur
27 Maimbung District Hospital (new Level II) Sulu
28 Sulu Provincial Hospital upgrade to Level II Sulu
29 Buluan District Hospital upgrade to Level II Maguindanao

79 Maluso General Hospital (new Level I) Basilan
80 Montaner Memorial Hospital upgrade Not specified

Research relevance: If your research concerns health infrastructure, hospital authority, or health service delivery in a specific province, check whether a hospital BAA applies to your geographic area. These BAAs define bed capacity, service levels, and funding — data you may need.

7.3 Determining Whether a BAA Is Still Good Law

A BAA may have been amended, superseded, or struck down. Before citing any BAA as authority, verify its status using this three-step process:

Step 1: Check the Amendment Chain.

Scan the full BAA index for any later BAA that expressly amends your target BAA. The following amendment relationships exist as of February 2026:

Original BAA Amending BAA What Changed
BAA 3 (GAA 2020) BAA 14 Extended fund availability
BAA 13 (Admin Code) BAA 37 Changed seat of government
BAA 23 (GAA 2022) BAA 33 Extended fund availability
BAA 32 (GAA 2023) BAA 52 Extended fund availability
BAA 35 (Electoral Code) BAA 87, BAA 88 Removed NOTA; other amendments
BAA 54 (Datu Sinsuat Balabaran) BAA 70 Amended Section 5
BAA 55 (Sheik Abas Hamza) BAA 71 Amended Section 5
BAA 58 (Parliamentary Districts) BAA 77 Reconstituted districts
MMAA 17 (Muslim Holidays) BAA 39 Repealed MMAA 17 entirely
MMAA 164 (Madrasah Grad Ed) BAA 50 Repealed MMAA 164 entirely

Step 2: Check for judicial invalidation.

The Supreme Court can strike down a BAA for unconstitutionality or for exceeding the BOL’s grant of authority. As of February 2026, the most significant judicial action concerns BAA 58 and BAA 77, both invalidated in October 2025. BAA 86 was enacted as the replacement.

Check Supreme Court decisions and Philippine legal databases (LawPhil, Supreme Court E-Library) for any ruling affecting the BAA you are citing.

Step 3: Check for implied repeal.

A later BAA may not expressly repeal an earlier one but may cover the same subject so comprehensively that the earlier BAA is impliedly superseded. Implied repeal is disfavored in statutory construction — courts presume both laws can coexist unless they are irreconcilably conflicting. But if the later BAA contains an “all inconsistent laws are hereby repealed” clause, provisions of the earlier BAA that conflict with the later one are superseded.

7.4 Two Interpretive Rules for Resolving BAA Conflicts

When two BAAs appear to conflict, two rules of statutory construction determine which prevails:

Lex posterior derogat legi priori — the later law prevails over the earlier law.

When Parliament enacts a BAA that is inconsistent with an earlier BAA on the same subject, the later BAA prevails. This is the standard rule for legislative succession.

Example: BAA 58 created parliamentary districts. BAA 77 amended BAA 58 by reconstituting those districts. BAA 77, as the later law, prevailed over BAA 58’s original district configuration. (Both were then struck down by the Supreme Court, and BAA 86 replaced them — demonstrating that lex posterior applies in sequence.)

Lex specialis derogat legi generali — the specific law prevails over the general law.

When a general BAA (like BAA 13, the Administrative Code) and a specific BAA (like BAA 5, creating BAGO) address the same subject, the specific BAA prevails on matters it specifically addresses. The general BAA still applies to matters the specific BAA does not cover.

Example: BAA 13, Book IV, Title II, Sections 10-24 describe the Bangsamoro Attorney-General’s Office in detail. BAA 5 is the standalone creating law for BAGO. If BAA 5 provides a power or function that BAA 13 does not mention, BAA 5 governs. If BAA 13 provides administrative detail not found in BAA 5, BAA 13 supplements. If the two conflict on a specific point, BAA 5 — as the specific law — prevails.

Combined application: When the two rules point in the same direction (the later law is also the more specific law), the answer is clear. When they point in opposite directions (the later law is the general one, and the earlier law is the specific one), courts generally favor lex specialis — the specific law prevails — unless the later general law contains language clearly intended to supersede the earlier specific provision.

7.5 The BAA Research Process

7.5.1 Start With the Question, Not With BAA 13

A common mistake is opening BAA 13 and scrolling until something looks relevant. This is slow and unreliable. Instead, start with your research question and use it to navigate directly to the right provisions.

From Chapter 2’s methodology, you already have: - A framed research question (Step 1 — Issue Analysis) - An understanding of the legal landscape (Step 2 — Landscape Review) - The correct tier identified through the national law relationship test (Chapter 3)

If the national law relationship test confirms you are in enumerated Bangsamoro power territory (no national law consistency required), your systematic source search at Tiers 3 and 4 follows this sequence:

7.5.2 The Five-Point BAA Search Sequence

Point 1: BAA 13 — Find the relevant ministry.

Identify which ministry holds the mandate for your research subject. Use the Book VI Title index (Section 6.1.5 above). Read the ministry’s Declaration of Policy, Mandate, and Powers and Functions in that order.

Point 2: BAA 13 — Cross-reference with other Books.

After identifying the ministry, check whether your question also touches: - The Chief Minister’s executive authority (Book IV) - Intergovernmental coordination (Book II) - Parliamentary legislative authority (Book III) - Administrative services or civil service rules (Book VI, Titles XVIII-XIX) - Government corporations (Book VI, Title XX)

Point 3: Check for a standalone creating BAA.

Using the creating/charter BAA table (Section 6.2.2, Category 1), determine whether the relevant entity was created by a standalone BAA. If so, read that BAA for additional powers and operational provisions.

Point 4: Check for a comprehensive code BAA.

Using the code BAA table (Section 6.2.2, Category 2), determine whether Parliament has enacted a comprehensive code on your subject. If so, the code is your primary source — not BAA 13’s summary provisions.

Point 5: Search for sector-specific BAAs.

Scan the full BAA index for any BAA that addresses your specific topic. Use the category tables in Section 6.2.2 as your guide, but also do a keyword search of the full index — a BAA’s relevance may not be obvious from its category alone.

7.5.3 Documenting Your BAA Research

As you work through the five-point sequence, build a BAA Research Tracker — a table that records which BAAs you have checked, what you found, and whether the provision is current:

BAA Relevant? Provision(s) What It Says Current? Notes
13 Yes Book VI, Title III, Sec. 2-3 MENRE mandate and powers Yes (amended by BAA 37 on seat of government only) Primary authority for environmental mandate
31 Yes Sections 1-5 Creates Bangsamoro Sustainable Development Board Yes Board, not a code — limited to sustainable development planning
35 No — Electoral Code — not relevant to environmental research — Skipped
… … … … … …

This tracker becomes part of your Research Memorandum (Chapter 12). It documents your diligence and ensures no BAA is overlooked.

7.6 Shari’ah Checkpoint

Before concluding your Tier 3 and 4 research, ask these questions:

Does BAA 13 reference Shari’ah in the relevant ministry’s provisions?

Several BAA 13 provisions incorporate Shari’ah considerations. Book IV, Title II, Section 17(3)(d) requires the BAGO’s Legal Research and Opinion Division to “formulate and review policies on Shari’ah matters to ensure compliance with Shari’ah rules and principles.”6 Book I, Chapter 5, Section 17 defines public office as a “sacred public trust” and requires public officers to “serve as ‘khalifa’” — a concept rooted in Islamic governance.7

Does any relevant BAA invoke Shari’ah explicitly?

Some comprehensive code BAAs include Shari’ah provisions. Search for the terms “Shari’ah,” “Islamic,” “Halal,” “khalifa,” and “moral governance” within any BAA you cite.

Does the Bangsamoro Darul Ifta’ have a relevant ruling?

BAA 13, Book I, Chapter 3, Section 14 provides that the Bangsamoro Information Office shall translate relevant documents in Arabic “in coordination with the Bangsamoro Darul Ifta’.”8 The Darul Ifta’ issues fatwas (religious rulings) that may bear on your research topic. These are not BAAs, but they carry persuasive authority within the Bangsamoro governance framework.

Does your research subject involve moral governance?

BAA 13’s Introductory Provisions, Section 3(j) commits the Bangsamoro Government to “maintain honesty, integrity, transparency and accountability” and take “positive and effective measures against graft and corruption.”9 The Oath of Moral Governance in Book I, Chapter 7, Section 24 is mandatory for all public officers.10 If your research touches procurement, contracting, personnel management, or any area susceptible to corruption concerns, the moral governance framework is a relevant overlay.

7.7 Worked Example: Researching Environmental Regulation Authority in BARMM

A policy advisor asks: “Does the Bangsamoro Government have authority to enact comprehensive environmental regulations? What legal authority exists now, and what gaps remain?”

Step 1: Frame the Question

Using Chapter 2’s three-part frame: - Subject: Environmental regulation - Jurisdiction: BARMM (checking national law applicability) - Action: Determine whether comprehensive regulatory authority exists and identify gaps

Step 2: National Law Relationship (Chapter 3)

Check BOL Article V, Section 2. Item (x) lists: “Environment, parks, forest management, wildlife, and nature reserves conservation.”11 This is an enumerated Bangsamoro power with no national law consistency requirement. BAAs are the controlling authority.

Step 3: BAA 13 — Find the Relevant Ministry (Point 1)

Open Book VI. Title III is “Environment, Natural Resources and Energy.” Read:

  • Section 1 (Declaration of Policies): Three policy pillars — natural resource development, renewable energy, and forest/coastal conservation.12
  • Section 2 (Mandate): MENRE is “primarily responsible for the exploration, utilization, management, conservation, protection and sustainable development of the region’s environment, natural resources and energy sources.”13
  • Section 3 (Powers and Functions): 19 specific powers, including recommending environmental laws, promulgating rules on pollution control, regulating natural resource development, and formulating conservation strategies.14

Finding: MENRE has the institutional mandate and 19 enumerated powers covering environmental regulation. BAA 13 establishes the ministry’s authority but does not itself constitute a comprehensive environment code.

Step 4: Cross-Reference with Other Books (Point 2)

  • Book II, Section 2(f): The Bangsamoro Sustainable Development Board is listed as an IGR body.15
  • Book IV: No specific environmental provisions in the Chief Minister’s powers.

Step 5: Check for Standalone Creating BAAs (Point 3)

  • BAA 31 creates the Bangsamoro Sustainable Development Board (BSDB). This is a board — a coordinating and advisory body, not a regulatory agency. It does not constitute an environment code or a comprehensive regulatory framework. It addresses sustainable development planning, not environmental law enforcement.16

Step 6: Check for Comprehensive Code BAAs (Point 4)

Search the BAA index for any BAA titled “Environment Code,” “Environmental Protection,” or similar. Result: None found. No comprehensive environment code has been enacted as of February 2026.

Step 7: Search for Sector-Specific BAAs (Point 5)

Scan the full 89-BAA index for any BAA touching environmental subjects:

  • BAA 6 (Socio-Economic Development Planning System) — touches environment only as part of broader planning
  • BAA 31 (Bangsamoro Sustainable Development Board) — already checked
  • BAA 60, 61 (memorial sites) — not environmental regulation

Finding: No sector-specific environment BAA exists.

Step 8: Check National Laws Applying Suppletorily

Since no comprehensive Bangsamoro environment code exists, national environmental laws apply suppletorily within BARMM:

  • RA 9729 (Climate Change Act) and its IRR
  • RA 9003 (Ecological Solid Waste Management Act)
  • RA 9275 (Philippine Clean Water Act)
  • RA 8749 (Philippine Clean Air Act)
  • PD 1586 (Environmental Impact Statement System)

These national laws operate in the absence of Bangsamoro environmental legislation but would yield to a future BAA on subjects within the BOL’s enumerated powers.

Step 9: Shari’ah Checkpoint

Islamic stewardship (khalifah) principles support environmental protection. The concept of humanity as trustee of the earth is consistent with MENRE’s conservation mandate. No specific Darul Ifta’ ruling on environmental regulation was found, but the principle strengthens the policy basis for comprehensive legislation.

Research Conclusion

Authority exists but has not been fully exercised. The BOL grants enumerated power over environment and natural resources (Art. V, Sec. 2(x)), with no national law consistency requirement. BAA 13 establishes MENRE with a mandate and 19 enumerated powers. BAA 31 creates an advisory board for sustainable development. But no comprehensive Bangsamoro Environment Code has been enacted. This is a legislative gap. National environmental laws apply suppletorily. The Bangsamoro Parliament has the authority to enact a comprehensive environment code — it has simply not yet done so.

BAA Research Tracker for This Example

BAA Relevant? Provision(s) Finding Current?
13 Yes Book VI, Title III, Sec. 1-3 MENRE mandate and 19 powers Yes
6 Marginal General provisions Development planning, not environmental regulation Yes
31 Yes Creating provisions Advisory board for sustainable development — not a code Yes
60 No — Memorial site —
61 No — Memorial eco-park —
All others No — No environmental subject matter —

7.8 Researching Bills and Proposed Resolutions

Your research at Tiers 3 and 4 should not stop at enacted BAAs. The legislative landscape includes filed bills and proposed resolutions that have not yet been enacted. These are not law, but they are research-relevant for three reasons:

  1. They signal legislative intent. A filed bill tells you that Parliament is considering acting on a subject. If your research identifies a gap (like the environment code gap above), a filed bill may show that the gap is about to be filled.

  2. They may affect your timeline. If you are drafting a policy that relies on a legislative gap (e.g., applying national law because no BAA exists), a pending bill may close that gap and change your legal analysis.

  3. They provide comparative text. A filed bill’s provisions show how Parliament’s committees are thinking about a subject. This is useful context even if the bill has not been enacted.

Where to find bills and resolutions:

  • The Bangsamoro Parliament’s official records (Committee Secretariat)
  • The bill drafting process, which tracks the legislative agenda
  • Published indexes of filed bills (e.g., the Parliament’s website, if available)

How to document them: In your Research Memorandum, list pending legislation in a separate section labeled “Pending Legislation” — distinct from enacted law. Note the bill number, title, author, committee referral, and status (filed, in committee, plenary, etc.). Never cite a pending bill as enacted law.

7.9 Common Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Confusing BAA Numbers

BAA numbers are sequential, not topical. BAA 25 is not the Education Code — it upgrades the Datu Blah Sinsuat District Hospital. BAA 60 is not BEZA — it declares a memorial site. BAA 11 is not the Education Code — it defines the power of appointment.

Prevention: Always verify BAA numbers against the official index. Never assume a BAA’s content from its number alone. The BAA Quick-Reference (categorized by domain) is faster than the sequential index for topical searches.

Pitfall 2: Citing BAA 13 Without Checking for a Standalone BAA

BAA 13 provides summary provisions for entities that have their own creating laws. If you cite BAA 13’s description of BAGO without also checking BAA 5, you may miss critical powers or functions that only BAA 5 provides.

Prevention: For any entity referenced in BAA 13, check whether a standalone creating BAA exists. The table in Section 6.1.4 is your quick reference.

Pitfall 3: Assuming a BAA Is Still Good Law

BAAs can be amended (BAA 37 amending BAA 13), superseded (BAA 86 replacing BAA 58/77), or struck down (Supreme Court invalidating BAA 58 and BAA 77). Citing an invalidated BAA as authority is a research failure.

Prevention: Run the three-step verification (Section 7.3) for every BAA you cite: check amendments, check judicial decisions, check for implied repeal.

Pitfall 4: Treating the Mandate as Sufficient Legal Authority

A ministry’s mandate in BAA 13 says what it exists to do. The powers and functions say what it can do. Citing only the mandate to justify a specific action is legally insufficient — the action must fall within one of the enumerated powers or be necessarily implied from them.

Prevention: Always cite the specific power or function, not just the mandate, when answering questions about institutional authority.

Pitfall 5: Missing Legislative Gaps

Not finding a BAA on your subject is itself a finding. If the BOL grants power but no BAA has been enacted, you have identified a legislative gap. Document it. National law applies suppletorily in the gap. Your research memorandum should state: “No BAA has been enacted on this subject as of [date]. National law applies.”

Prevention: Treat absence of legislation as a conclusion, not as a failure to find something.

Pitfall 6: Ignoring Appropriations BAAs

The GAA’s General Provisions section creates binding rules for the fiscal year — spending limits, earmarks, restrictions on fund transfers. If your research concerns government spending or program funding, the latest GAA is a legal source, not just a budget document.

Prevention: When your research touches any financial question, add the current GAA (BAA 85 for FY 2026) and the Budget System Act (BAA 84) to your BAA Research Tracker.

Pitfall 7: Conflating BAAs with National Laws That Sound Similar

BAA 17 is the Bangsamoro Civil Service Code. It is not the same as the national Civil Service law (EO 292, Book V). BAA 82 is the Bangsamoro Labor and Employment Code. It is not the same as the national Labor Code (PD 442). These operate in parallel, and the BOL determines which prevails when they conflict.

Prevention: In your Research Memorandum, always specify whether you are citing a BAA or a national law. Use the full formal title on first reference.

7.11 Quality Checklist

Before finalizing your Tier 3 and 4 research, verify each item:

  • ☐ BAA 13 checked. You identified and read the relevant Book, Title, and sections of BAA 13.
  • ☐ Ministry mandate AND powers/functions distinguished. You cited specific powers, not just the broad mandate.
  • ☐ Standalone creating BAAs checked. For any entity referenced in BAA 13, you verified whether a standalone BAA provides additional authority.
  • ☐ Comprehensive code BAAs checked. You determined whether a comprehensive code exists for your subject (and documented the gap if none exists).
  • ☐ Sector-specific BAAs checked. You searched the full BAA index for any BAA relevant to your topic.
  • ☐ Appropriations BAAs checked (if your question involves funding or spending).
  • ☐ Amendment status verified. Every BAA you cite has been verified as current using the three-step process.
  • ☐ Legislative gaps documented. If no BAA covers your subject, you stated this as a finding and identified applicable national laws.
  • ☐ Bills and resolutions checked. You searched for pending legislation relevant to your research topic.
  • ☐ Shari’ah checkpoint completed. You checked for Shari’ah references in relevant BAAs and Darul Ifta’ rulings.
  • ☐ BAA Research Tracker completed. Your tracker documents every BAA checked, with findings and currency status.
  • ☐ No fabricated BAA numbers. Every BAA number you cite corresponds to an actual enacted BAA verified against the official index.

Key Takeaways

  1. BAA 13 is your first stop for institutional authority. It creates most ministries and offices and defines their mandates and powers. Always start here.

  2. Distinguish mandate from powers and functions. The mandate says why the entity exists. The powers and functions say what it can legally do. Cite the powers, not just the mandate.

  3. Always check for standalone creating BAAs. BAA 13 is the general framework; standalone BAAs provide specific detail. Use both.

  4. Categorize BAAs before searching. Knowing the six categories (creating, codes, sector-specific, appropriations, municipalities, symbols) helps you navigate 89 BAAs efficiently.

  5. Verify every BAA is current. Check for amendments, judicial invalidation, and implied repeal before citing any BAA as authority.

  6. Document legislative gaps. Not finding a BAA is itself a finding. The absence of comprehensive legislation on a subject within the BOL’s enumerated powers is a gap worth reporting.

  7. Lex posterior and lex specialis resolve conflicts. Later law prevails over earlier law. Specific law prevails over general law. When the two rules conflict, specific law generally wins.
