Back to Table of Contents
08
National Laws and Implementing Frameworks

Chapter 8 — National Laws and Implementing Frameworks

Chapters 4 and 5 covered the Constitution and BOL. Chapters 6 and 7 covered BAA 13 and other Bangsamoro Autonomy Acts. This chapter covers two tiers that originate outside the Bangsamoro Parliament: national laws enacted by the Philippine Congress (Tier 5) and executive issuances from both the Chief Minister and national officials (Tier 6). Jurisprudence (Tier 7) is treated separately in Chapter 10.

These tiers are where most researchers make their costliest mistakes. National laws get ignored because “BARMM is autonomous.” Executive issuances get treated as optional guidance. Each error can invalidate an entire research memorandum.

The challenge at Tiers 5-6 is not finding the sources — they are generally more accessible than BAAs. The challenge is determining whether and how they apply within the Bangsamoro. A national law that applies everywhere else in the Philippines may not apply in BARMM. This chapter teaches you to navigate these determinations systematically.

8.1 Purpose

After completing this chapter, you will be able to:

  1. Determine whether a national law applies in BARMM using the three-mode applicability test (direct, suppletory, express adoption)
  2. Search for national laws across four online databases and evaluate which version is authoritative
  3. Distinguish four types of executive issuances and identify their legal limits

The skills in this chapter build directly on the national law relationship framework from Chapter 3. If you have not completed the national law relationship test for your research question (Section 3.3), go back and do it now. The result — whether the power requires national law consistency, requires no national law consistency, or is a residual national power (or a legislative gap exists) — determines how you approach every source at Tiers 5-6.

8.2 Tier 5: National Laws

8.2.1 Three Ways National Laws Apply in BARMM

Not every Republic Act applies in BARMM. Not every Republic Act is excluded. The Bangsamoro is autonomous, not independent. The BOL creates a system where national laws interact with Bangsamoro legislation through three distinct mechanisms. Your research must identify which mechanism applies — because the legal effect differs for each one.

Mode 1: Direct Application

A national law applies directly when the subject falls outside the BOL’s 55 enumerated powers — that is, when the subject is a residual national power under Article V, Section 1.1 In these areas, the Bangsamoro Parliament has no legislative competence. National law governs fully and exclusively.

Examples of direct application: - National defense and security — The Armed Forces of the Philippines and the Philippine National Police operate under national law within BARMM. The Bangsamoro Government does not have military or police powers (though Art. XI, Sec. 4 provides for police coordination).2 - Foreign affairs — Treaty-making, diplomatic relations, and consular operations are exclusively national. - Immigration and citizenship — The Bureau of Immigration enforces national immigration law throughout BARMM. Parliament cannot create a separate immigration regime. - Currency and monetary policy — The Bangko Sentral ng Pilipinas operates under national law. BARMM cannot issue currency or set monetary policy. - Customs and tariff — Customs operations at BARMM ports follow national customs law, though Art. XII, Secs. 15-16 include Bureau of Customs collections in the block grant formula.

How to identify direct application: If your national law relationship test (Chapter 3, Section 3.3) returned “Residual National Power” at Step 1 — the subject is not within the 55 enumerated powers — then any national law on that subject applies directly. Your research at Tier 5 is straightforward: find the applicable national law and apply it.

Mode 2: Suppletory Application

A national law applies suppletorily when the subject is within the 55 enumerated powers (Art. V, Sec. 2) but the Parliament has not yet enacted a BAA on that subject. The national law fills the gap until the Parliament exercises its power.3

The concept comes from legal principle: when a specialized law is silent on a matter, the general law applies to fill the silence. In the BARMM context, this means national laws serve as the default framework for any enumerated power that Parliament has not yet legislated on.

Examples of suppletory application: - Cooperatives — Art. V, Sec. 2(k) lists “Cooperatives and social entrepreneurship” as an enumerated power where the BOL is silent on national law consistency. No BAA specifically codifying cooperative law has been enacted as of March 2026. RA 9520 (Philippine Cooperative Code of 2008) therefore applies suppletorily within BARMM.4 - Labor — Art. V, Sec. 2(jj) lists “Labor, employment, and occupation” as an enumerated power. BAA 82 (Bangsamoro Labor and Employment Code), enacted January 2026, is the regional labor framework. The national Labor Code (PD 442, as amended) applies suppletorily where BAA 82 is silent. - Tourism — Art. V, Sec. 2(yy) lists “Tourism development” as an enumerated power. BAA 13 creates the Ministry of Trade, Investments, and Tourism (MTIT), but no standalone tourism code exists. RA 9593 (Tourism Act of 2009) applies suppletorily.

How to identify suppletory application: If your national law relationship test returned “Legislative Gap” at Step 3 — the subject is an enumerated power (Art. V, Sec. 2) where no national law consistency is required, but no BAA has been enacted — the applicable national law fills the gap. Your research must cover both Tier 4 (confirming the gap) and Tier 5 (identifying the suppletory national law).

The suppletory application finding is itself a research output. When you discover a legislative gap filled by suppletory national law, document it explicitly in your Research Memorandum. This is valuable institutional knowledge. It tells decision-makers: Parliament has the power to legislate on this subject but has not done so. Until it does, this national law governs.

Mode 3: Express Adoption

A national law applies through express adoption when the Parliament enacts a BAA that explicitly incorporates or aligns with a national framework. The BAA is the controlling authority, but it draws its substantive provisions from the national law — either by copying the framework, referencing it, or declaring consistency with it.

Examples of express adoption: - BAA 84 and national budget law — BAA 84 (Budget System Act) establishes the Bangsamoro budget process consistent with the BOL’s requirement that the budget conform to “existing laws, rules and regulations of the National Government” (Art. VII, Sec. 28).6 - Civil service legislation — When the Parliament legislates on civil service matters, Art. VII, Sec. 39 requires consistency with national civil service laws. BAA 17 (Civil Service Code) constitutes express adoption of the national framework to the extent required by the BOL.7 - Procurement — RA 12009 (Government Procurement Reform Act) applies directly to all BARMM procurement because no regional procurement BAA has been enacted. Art. VII, Sec. 28 requires budget consistency with national laws, and COA audits all BARMM procurement against RA 12009 standards. This is both a legislative gap (Mode 2) and a national law consistency area — RA 12009 would apply even if a BAA were enacted, because procurement is closely linked to the budget consistency requirement.5

How to identify express adoption: Check the BAA’s text. Does it reference a specific national law? Does it use language like “consistent with,” “in accordance with,” “pursuant to,” or “shall apply suppletorily”? If so, your research must cover both the BAA (Tier 4) and the adopted national law (Tier 5), and your Comparative Matrix must map the relationship between them.

The Applicability Decision Flowchart

For any national law you encounter during research, apply this test:

Step 1: Is the subject a residual national power? - YES → National law applies directly. No BAA can override it. Stop here. - NO → The subject is within the 55 enumerated powers. Proceed to Step 2.

Step 2: Does the BOL require consistency with national law on this subject? - YES → National law consistency is required. National law sets the floor or ceiling. The BAA must be consistent with it. Both apply. Stop here. - NO → Proceed to Step 3.

Step 3: Has Parliament enacted a BAA on this subject? - YES → Check whether the BAA expressly adopts or references the national law. - BAA expressly adopts → Both apply; BAA is the controlling authority but national law fills gaps (Mode 3). - BAA does not reference the national law → The BAA governs within BARMM for this subject. The national law does not apply. - NO → Legislative gap. National law applies suppletorily until Parliament acts (Mode 2).

8.2.2 Key Universal National Laws

Some national laws affect nearly every MOA regardless of the national law relationship determination. These are laws of general application — they apply to all government entities, including BARMM, because they address cross-cutting obligations (ethics, anti-corruption, gender, data protection) rather than sector-specific subjects covered by the 55 enumerated powers.

Include these in your research whenever your question involves government operations, personnel conduct, procurement, or public service delivery:

National Law Short Title Why It Matters for BARMM
RA 6713 Code of Conduct and Ethical Standards for Public Officials and Employees Applies to all public officials including BARMM appointees. Defines norms of conduct, disclosure requirements, and prohibited acts.
RA 3019 Anti-Graft and Corrupt Practices Act Applies to all public officers. Defines corrupt practices and penalties. COA and Ombudsman enforce this within BARMM.
RA 12009 New Government Procurement Act Applies directly to BARMM (no regional procurement BAA enacted). COA audits all BARMM procurement against RA 12009 standards.
RA 9710 Magna Carta of Women Requires at least 5% of total budget for gender-responsive programs. See also BOL Art. XIII, Sec. 5 (gender equality).11
RA 10173 Data Privacy Act of 2012 Applies to all government processing of personal data, including BARMM civil registration, health records, and social welfare programs.
RA 11032 Ease of Doing Business and Efficient Government Service Delivery Act of 2018 Applies to all government agencies. Prescribes processing times and anti-red tape measures that BARMM agencies must follow.
RA 8371 Indigenous Peoples’ Rights Act (IPRA) BOL Art. IX, Sec. 3: IP rights “shall not be diminished.” NCIP retains jurisdiction over ancestral domain claims within BARMM.12
RA 7160 Local Government Code of 1991 BOL Art. VI, Sec. 10: privileges enjoyed by LGUs “shall not be diminished.” LGU powers, elections, and fiscal transfers operate under this framework.13

A practical warning: Do not assume that a national law applies simply because it is a “good idea” or “should” apply. Confirm the legal basis. A national law on agriculture, for example, does not automatically apply in BARMM because agriculture is under Art. V, Sec. 2(c) — an enumerated power where the BOL is silent on national law consistency. If the Parliament has enacted a BAA on the subject, that BAA controls. The eight laws listed above are universal because they address cross-cutting government obligations, not because of any blanket rule that national laws always prevail.

8.2.3 How to Search for National Laws

Four primary databases contain Philippine national legislation. Each has strengths and limitations.

1. Official Gazette (officialgazette.gov.ph)

  • Content: Published Republic Acts, Presidential issuances, proclamations
  • Strength: Official publication. Laws published here have legal force under the Tañada v. Tuvera doctrine — a law takes effect only after publication.11
  • Limitation: Not all laws are immediately uploaded. Search function is basic. Older laws may be missing.
  • Best for: Confirming official publication and effectivity dates

2. LawPhil Project (lawphil.net)

  • Content: Republic Acts, Presidential Decrees, Supreme Court decisions
  • Strength: Comprehensive collection of Philippine laws and jurisprudence. Free access. Well-organized by law number.
  • Limitation: Unofficial source. No guarantee that amendments are reflected in the posted text. Cross-reference against the Official Gazette for critical citations.
  • Best for: Initial searches and reading the full text of laws

3. Chan Robles Virtual Law Library (chanrobles.com)

  • Content: Statutes, jurisprudence, executive issuances, treaties
  • Strength: Extensive database. Often includes annotations and related cases.
  • Limitation: Unofficial. Some content is behind registration. Formatting can be inconsistent.
  • Best for: Finding related cases and annotations for a specific law

4. Supreme Court E-Library (elibrary.judiciary.gov.ph)

  • Content: Supreme Court decisions, court rules, some statutes
  • Strength: Official source for jurisprudence. Searchable by G.R. number, case title, date, and ponente.
  • Limitation: Primarily focused on jurisprudence rather than legislation. Search can be slow.
  • Best for: Finding the official text of Supreme Court decisions (Tier 7) and court-cited versions of statutes

5. Congress website (congress.gov.ph)

  • Content: Enrolled bills, committee reports, session proceedings
  • Strength: Official source for legislative history. Shows pending bills and their status.
  • Limitation: Search is limited. Not all enrolled bills are immediately posted. Historical records may be incomplete.
  • Best for: Tracking pending national legislation that may affect BARMM; understanding legislative intent through committee reports

Search strategy for national laws:

  1. Start with the RA number if you know it. Search by number across LawPhil or Chan Robles for the full text.
  2. If you do not know the RA number, search by subject on LawPhil or Chan Robles. Use the same search terms from your Issue Analysis (Chapter 2, Step 1).
  3. Check for amendments. Many RAs have been amended multiple times. RA 7160 (Local Government Code), for example, has been amended by RA 11292, RA 11463, and others. LawPhil sometimes indicates amendments; the Official Gazette does not always. Search for “amending RA [number]” to find subsequent amendments.
  4. Verify against the Official Gazette for effectivity dates and official publication status.
  5. Check the implementing rules. Many national laws have IRRs that provide the operational detail. Search for “[Law name] IRR” or “[Law name] implementing rules and regulations.”

The verification imperative. National law databases are unofficial sources maintained by private organizations (except the Official Gazette and the SC E-Library). Text may contain errors, omissions, or outdated versions. When a national law provision is critical to your research answer, verify the text against the Official Gazette version or a certified copy from the National Printing Office. Chapter 13 covers verification procedures in detail.

8.3 Tier 6: Executive Issuances

Executive issuances are the operational layer of the legal system. They translate legislation into action. A BAA or national law creates the authority; an executive issuance tells MOAs how to exercise that authority — what forms to use, what timelines to follow, what standards to apply.

For the legal researcher, Tier 6 matters because it contains the rules that people actually follow day-to-day. A Ministry officer building a program will refer to the IRR and the implementing MCs long before opening the BOL. Your research is incomplete if you stop at the BAA without checking whether an IRR, EO, or MC has been issued under it.

8.3.1 Types: EOs, IRRs, MCs, AOs

Four types of executive issuances operate within the BARMM legal framework:

Executive Orders (EOs)

Executive Orders are directives issued by the Chief Minister on regional matters, or by the President on national matters affecting BARMM.

  • Chief Minister EOs govern the internal operations of the Bangsamoro Government — reorganizing offices, creating task forces, directing ministries to implement specific policies. Example: OCM Executive Order No. 2, s. 2026, which established the implementation framework for the Mas Matatag na Bangsamoro Agenda (2026-2028).12
  • Presidential EOs affecting BARMM typically concern intergovernmental relations, administrative boundary adjustments, or national policies that extend to BARMM. Example: Executive Order No. 91, s. 2025, which realigned the Province of Sulu under Region IX following the Supreme Court’s ruling in Province of Sulu v. Medialdea.13

How EOs rank in the hierarchy: An EO cannot contradict the BAA, BOL provision, or national law it implements. It is subordinate to legislation. But within the space the legislation allows, an EO can direct how government agencies execute policy. Treat EOs as operational authority — they tell you how things work in practice, not whether they are permitted in law.

Implementing Rules and Regulations (IRRs)

IRRs provide the detailed implementation guidelines for a specific BAA or national law. When Parliament enacts a BAA, it often includes a provision directing the implementing ministry to promulgate an IRR within a specified period (typically 90 days).

Key rules for IRRs: - An IRR cannot expand the scope of the law it implements. If a BAA authorizes a ministry to regulate a specific activity, the IRR cannot extend that regulation to activities not covered by the BAA. This is the ultra vires doctrine — an IRR that goes beyond the law’s grant of authority is void to that extent.[^14] - An IRR cannot contradict the BAA. If the IRR imposes a requirement that conflicts with the BAA’s text, the BAA prevails. - An IRR interprets ambiguous provisions in the BAA. When the BAA uses general language (e.g., “appropriate safeguards”), the IRR defines what that means in practice (e.g., specific documentation requirements, timelines, penalties).

For your research: When you find a relevant BAA at Tier 4, always ask: Has an IRR been promulgated? If yes, the IRR may contain the operational detail that answers your specific question. If no IRR exists, that itself is a finding — it means the BAA’s implementation provisions may be incomplete.

Memorandum Circulars (MCs)

Memorandum Circulars are policy directives issued by the Office of the Chief Minister (OCM), ministries, or agencies. They communicate specific guidance on how to comply with existing laws and regulations.

  • OCM MCs apply government-wide — all ministries and agencies must comply.
  • Ministry MCs apply within the issuing ministry’s mandate. An MC from the Ministry of Finance, Budget, and Management (MFBM) applies to budget execution across government; an MC from the Ministry of Health (MOH) applies to health programs.

MCs occupy the lowest rung of binding authority. They can direct processes, set deadlines, and clarify requirements, but they cannot create new obligations not supported by a BAA or EO.

Administrative Orders (AOs)

Administrative Orders are internal directives within specific ministries. They govern internal procedures — personnel assignments, office workflows, reporting requirements — and do not typically create external legal obligations.

For your research: AOs are relevant when your question concerns the internal operations of a specific ministry. If you are researching how a ministry processes applications, for example, the AO governing that process is primary operational authority. AOs are rarely relevant to questions about legal authority or institutional mandates.

8.3.2 Validity and Publication Requirements

An executive issuance is not legally effective until it has been properly published. This is a constitutional requirement flowing from the Tañada v. Tuvera principle: Laws, to be valid and enforceable, must be published in the Official Gazette or in a newspaper of general circulation.[^15]

The BARMM publication rule: For Bangsamoro legislation, the standard effectivity clause provides that a BAA “shall take effect after fifteen (15) days from its publication in the Bangsamoro Gazette or in one (1) newspaper of regional circulation.”[^16] The same principle applies to executive issuances with legal effect — EOs, IRRs, and MCs that impose obligations on the public or on government agencies must be published.

The Bangsamoro Gazette is the official publication vehicle of the Bangsamoro Government. Publication in the Gazette satisfies the constitutional requirement.

What to check in your research: - Was the EO, IRR, or MC published? If not, it may not be legally effective. - When was it published? The issuance takes effect 15 days after publication (unless a different date is specified). - Where was it published? The Bangsamoro Gazette or a newspaper of regional circulation.

A practical challenge: Not all executive issuances are easily accessible. Unlike BAAs, which are compiled and indexed, EOs and MCs from individual ministries may not be centrally catalogued. When your research requires a specific executive issuance:

  1. Check the issuing ministry’s records — contact the ministry directly if needed.
  2. Check the OCM for Chief Minister EOs and government-wide MCs.
  3. Check the BTA Parliamentary Service — they may have copies if the issuance was submitted to Parliament.
  4. Search online — some ministries publish issuances on their websites or social media pages.

The difficulty of finding executive issuances is itself a finding. If a critical IRR has not been promulgated or published, that constitutes a gap in the implementation framework. Document it in your Research Memorandum.

8.3.3 Testing an Executive Issuance’s Validity

When you find an executive issuance relevant to your research question, apply this three-part validity test:

Test 1: Authority. Does the issuing official or office have the authority to issue this type of directive? - A Chief Minister EO on a regional matter: Valid authority. - A ministry MC on a matter within the ministry’s mandate: Valid authority. - A ministry MC on a matter outside its mandate: Lacks authority.

Test 2: Consistency. Does the issuance contradict the law it implements? - If an IRR imposes a requirement not found in the BAA: Potentially ultra vires. - If an MC contradicts an EO on the same subject: The EO prevails (higher authority). - If a Chief Minister EO contradicts a BAA: The BAA prevails.

Test 3: Publication. Has the issuance been published as required? - If the issuance imposes obligations on the public or government agencies and has not been published: May not be legally effective. - If the issuance concerns purely internal administrative matters (e.g., an AO on office hours): Publication may not be required.

If the issuance fails any of these tests, flag it in your Research Memorandum. A questionable executive issuance does not disappear from your research — you must still document it, but with a notation about the validity concern. The decision-maker needs to know that the issuance exists and that its legal standing is questionable.

8.4 Shari’ah Checkpoint

Tiers 5-6 present specific Shari’ah intersection points that the researcher must check:

Tier 5 — National laws and Shari’ah jurisdiction. National family law (the Family Code, EO 209) does not apply to Muslims whose personal law matters fall under Shari’ah court jurisdiction (PD 1083, BOL Art. X). When your research involves personal law (marriage, divorce, succession, property relations between Muslims), confirm whether Shari’ah law or national family law applies. The answer depends on the religious identity of the parties and the subject matter.8

Tier 5 — IPRA and traditional/customary law. RA 8371 protects indigenous peoples’ customary law, which may include elements of Shari’ah or pre-Islamic customary practices. When IP rights intersect with Shari’ah, the researcher must trace both legal tracks — they are not always consistent.

Tier 6 — Executive issuances implementing Shari’ah. Check whether any executive issuance establishes standards for halal certification, Islamic finance, waqf management, or zakat administration. These issuances may come from the Bangsamoro Darul-Ifta (the office of Islamic legal opinion) or the Ministry of Human Settlements and Development (for waqf properties).

The checkpoint rule remains: If your research question involves personal law, family relations, succession, property among Muslims, Islamic finance, halal standards, or waqf — Chapter 9 (Shari’ah and Customary Law) is not optional. Complete the Shari’ah analysis there before finalizing your Tier 5-6 research.

8.5 Worked Example: Does RA 9520 Apply in BARMM?

Research question: A cooperative development officer in the Ministry of Trade, Investments, and Tourism (MTIT) asks: “What law governs cooperatives in BARMM — is it the national Cooperative Code or do we have our own?”

Step 1: National Law Relationship Test

Apply the three-step test from Chapter 3, Section 3.3.

Is cooperatives within the 55 enumerated powers? Yes. Article V, Section 2(k) lists “Cooperatives and social entrepreneurship” as an enumerated power of the Bangsamoro Government.9

Does the BOL require national law consistency? No. Unlike civil service (Art. VII, Sec. 39), budget (Art. VII, Sec. 28), or elections (Art. IV, Sec. 4), the BOL does not contain a provision requiring cooperative legislation to be consistent with national law.

Has Parliament enacted a BAA on cooperatives? No. As of March 2026, no BAA specifically codifying cooperative law has been enacted. The 2nd Bangsamoro Development Plan (2023-2028) confirms this gap: “The pending codification of the Cooperative Code and Social Enterprise Code currently slows down the full operationalization and institutionalization of both Cooperatives and SE.”10

Classification: ENUMERATED POWER — NO NATIONAL LAW CONSISTENCY REQUIRED — LEGISLATIVE GAP.

Step 2: National Law Applicability Test

Applying the three-mode test from Section 8.2.1:

  • Direct application? No — cooperatives is not a residual national power; it is an enumerated Bangsamoro power (Art. V, Sec. 2(k)).
  • Power where BOL requires national law consistency? No — no BOL provision requires consistency with national cooperative law.
  • Legislative gap? Yes — Parliament has the enumerated power but has not exercised it.

Result: RA 9520 (Philippine Cooperative Code of 2008) applies suppletorily within BARMM until Parliament enacts a Bangsamoro Cooperative Code.

Step 3: Research Answer

RA 9520 (Philippine Cooperative Code of 2008) currently governs cooperatives in BARMM by suppletory application. The Bangsamoro Parliament has the enumerated power to legislate on cooperatives under Article V, Section 2(k) of the BOL, but has not yet enacted a Bangsamoro Cooperative Code. This legislative gap means:

  1. RA 9520 applies until Parliament acts — cooperative registration, governance standards, and regulatory oversight follow the national framework.
  2. The power to replace RA 9520 exists — Parliament can enact a Bangsamoro Cooperative Code that supersedes RA 9520 within the region.
  3. No BOL provision requires consistency with RA 9520 — unlike civil service or budget law, the Bangsamoro Cooperative Code does not need to mirror the national framework. Parliament has full legislative discretion.
  4. The BDP flags this as a priority — the 2nd BDP identifies the pending cooperative code as a constraint on sector development.

8.6 Common Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Assuming national law does not apply because “BARMM is autonomous.”

This is a critical Tier 5 error. Autonomy does not mean independence. National laws apply directly in residual power areas, suppletorily in legislative gap areas, and as the floor or ceiling in areas where the BOL requires national law consistency. The only situation where a national law does not apply at all is when Parliament has enacted a BAA on a subject that is (a) within the 55 enumerated powers and (b) not subject to a BOL consistency requirement — and even then, laws of general application (anti-graft, data privacy, gender) still apply.

Pitfall 2: Assuming national law always applies because “the Philippines is a unitary state.”

The opposite error. Some researchers default to national law because they are unfamiliar with the Bangsamoro framework or because national law is easier to find. But in enumerated power areas where a BAA exists and no national law consistency is required, the BAA governs. Citing the national Labor Code without mentioning BAA 82 (Bangsamoro Labor and Employment Code), for example, is incomplete research — BAA 82 is the controlling regional framework for labor matters.

Pitfall 3: Citing an IRR without checking the BAA it implements.

An IRR derives its authority from the law it implements. If the BAA has been amended, the IRR may be outdated. If the IRR imposes requirements not found in the BAA, those requirements may be ultra vires. Always trace the IRR back to its parent law and verify consistency.

Pitfall 4: Missing the suppletory application finding.

When no BAA exists on an enumerated power, many researchers simply stop: “No BAA found.” That is an incomplete finding. The complete finding is: “No BAA found; national law applies suppletorily; this is a legislative gap.” The suppletory application is an affirmative legal determination, not a void. Document it as such.

8.7 Template: National Law Applicability Checklist

Use this template for every national law you encounter during research. It implements the three-mode applicability test from Section 8.2.1.

StepQuestionFindingResult
1Is the subject a residual national power? (Not within the 55 enumerated powers of Art. V, Sec. 2)[Identify whether the subject matches any enumerated power]YES / NO
If YES → National law applies directly. Document and stop.
2Does the BOL require consistency with national law? (Check: Art. VII Sec. 39, Art. VII Sec. 28, Art. IV Sec. 4, Art. XII Sec. 2, Art. IX Sec. 3)[Which provision, if any?]YES / NO

8.8 Quality Checklist

Before relying on your Tier 5-6 research, confirm:

  • ☐ Every national law cited has been classified using the three-mode applicability test (direct, suppletory, express adoption)
  • ☐ Cross-cutting national laws (RA 6713, RA 3019, RA 9710, RA 10173, RA 11032, RA 8371, RA 7160) have been checked for relevance
  • ☐ For each suppletory application finding, the legislative gap has been documented and flagged
  • ☐ All executive issuances have been tested for authority, consistency, and publication
  • ☐ IRRs have been traced back to their parent BAA or national law and checked for consistency
  • ☐ The Shari’ah checkpoint has been completed for Tiers 5-6
  • ☐ The National Law Applicability Checklist has been completed for each relevant national law
  • ☐ All citations follow Feliciano 10th Edition format

Back to Table of Contents
09
Shari'ah and Customary Law

Chapter 9 — Shari’ah and Customary Law

Every chapter in this guidebook has ended with a Shari’ah Checkpoint — a single question: Does this research question have a Shari’ah dimension? If you answered “yes” at any checkpoint, this chapter tells you what to do next.

Shari’ah is not a niche topic that only affects family law disputes. It is a parallel legal dimension woven into the constitutional fabric of the Bangsamoro. The BOL dedicates 20 sections across Article X to the justice system, Shari’ah courts, traditional law, and alternative dispute resolution. PD 1083 — the Code of Muslim Personal Laws, enacted in 1977 — remains the primary codification of Muslim personal law in the Philippines. The Shari’ah court system forms part of the Philippine judicial system under Supreme Court supervision. Any legal researcher working in BARMM will encounter Shari’ah questions, whether the research involves inheritance, family property, commercial contracts between Muslims, or the constitutional limits of Parliament’s legislative power over Islamic law.

AI in Practice

AI tools have limited understanding of Islamic jurisprudence. They can locate BOL Art. X provisions and PD 1083 sections, but they cannot interpret Shari’ah. This chapter has a higher human verification burden than any other.

What AI does: Searches BOL Art. X (Secs. 1-20) and PD 1083 for applicable provisions. Identifies jurisdictional boundaries (which Shari’ah court has authority). Locates the monetary thresholds for Circuit vs. District Court. What you do: All Shari’ah interpretation. AI finds the enacted provisions; you (or a qualified Islamic legal scholar) determine what Shari’ah requires. Arabic legal terms in AI output must be verified against scholarly sources. Watch for: AI frequently presents fiqh concepts (mudarabah, sulh, ta’zir) as if they were enacted BARMM law. They are not — no BAA codifies most Arabic legal terms. Label fiqh as scholarly, not statutory.

This chapter addresses a specific question that other treatments of Shari’ah in BARMM governance do not cover: how to research Shari’ah legal questions systematically, using the same five-step methodology from Chapter 2 while navigating the unique features of Islamic legal sources.

9.1 Purpose

After completing this chapter, you will be able to:

  1. Identify when a research question has a Shari’ah dimension and determine the scope of that dimension
  2. Navigate BOL Article X and PD 1083 to find the applicable Shari’ah provisions
  3. Apply the jurisdictional matrix to determine which Shari’ah court has authority over a specific case
  4. Distinguish between the principal and secondary sources of Shari’ah as defined by the BOL
  5. Use the Maqasid al-Shari’ah framework to assess whether proposed legislation aligns with Islamic objectives
  6. Research traditional and tribal law questions involving indigenous peoples within BARMM
  7. Locate Shari’ah legal sources — from local codifications to international Islamic law standards

9.2 Shari’ah as a Parallel Legal Dimension

Chapter 3 introduced the seven-tier legal hierarchy and the three relationships to national law. Shari’ah does not sit at any single tier. It cuts across all of them.

Tier 1 — Constitution. Article X, Section 15 of the 1987 Constitution recognizes regional autonomy, including the power to establish justice systems appropriate to the cultural heritage of autonomous regions. Article X, Section 20 grants autonomous regions legislative powers “subject to the provisions of this Constitution and national laws.”1

Tier 2 — BOL. Article X of RA 11054 dedicates 20 sections to the Bangsamoro justice system. Sections 1-16 cover Shari’ah courts, their jurisdiction, sources of law, qualifications of judges, and the power of Parliament to legislate. Sections 17-18 cover traditional and tribal justice. Sections 19-20 cover regular courts and alternative dispute resolution.2

Tier 3 — BAA 13. The Bangsamoro Administrative Code establishes the institutional framework within which Shari’ah-related agencies operate, including the Bangsamoro Darul-Ifta.

Tier 4 — Other BAAs. Parliament may enact BAAs that codify Shari’ah provisions — on Islamic finance, halal regulation, waqf (endowment), zakat (alms), or family law matters not covered by PD 1083. As of March 2026, no comprehensive Shari’ah-specific BAA has been enacted, but the legislative authority exists under Article X, Section 4.

Tier 5 — National law. Presidential Decree No. 1083 (Code of Muslim Personal Laws, 1977) is the existing national codification of Muslim personal law. It covers marriage, divorce, inheritance, family relations, and property rights for Muslims. The BOL explicitly references PD 1083 in multiple provisions, and Article X, Section 4 grants Parliament power to legislate on matters “not provided for” under PD 1083.3 This means PD 1083 continues to apply for subjects it covers.

Tier 6 — Executive issuances. Ministerial circulars and administrative orders may implement Shari’ah-related provisions of existing law.

Tier 7 — Jurisprudence. Supreme Court decisions define the boundaries of Shari’ah court jurisdiction. Because all Shari’ah courts are part of the Philippine judicial system, their decisions are reviewable by the Supreme Court on questions of law.4

The practical implication: When your research question has a Shari’ah dimension, you do not abandon the seven-tier methodology. You apply it — but you add a Shari’ah column to your search at every tier. The hierarchy still determines which source prevails. The national law relationship analysis still applies. The difference is that you are now tracking an additional legal stream that runs parallel to the secular one.

9.3 BOL Article X: The Constitutional Framework

Article X of the BOL is titled “The Justice System in the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region” (Sections 1-16 cover Shari’ah; Sections 17-18 cover tribal justice; Sections 19-20 cover regular courts and ADR). This is your primary source for Shari’ah research. Know it thoroughly.

9.3.1 Sources of Shari’ah

Section 3 defines the sources of Shari’ah recognized by the BOL:5

Principal Sources: - (a) Al-Qur’an — the holy book of Islam; the primary and supreme source - (b) Al-Sunnah — the traditions of Prophet Muhammad (SAW); the second primary source

Secondary Sources: - (a) Al-Ijma — consensus of Islamic scholars on a legal question - (b) Al-Qiyas — reasoning by analogy from established principles to new situations

This hierarchy of sources matters for research. When assessing whether a proposed BAA is consistent with Shari’ah, you check the principal sources first. If the Qur’an and Sunnah address the subject directly, that settles the question. If they do not address it directly, you look to Ijma and Qiyas — scholarly consensus and analogical reasoning.

A critical limitation for researchers: The BOL recognizes these sources but does not codify their content. The Qur’an is a religious text, not a statute. Applying it to a legal question requires Islamic jurisprudential expertise (fiqh). The legal researcher’s role is to identify when Shari’ah applies, map the jurisdictional framework, and flag questions that require consultation with Islamic legal scholars — not to independently interpret Quranic provisions. When your research reaches a point where Shari’ah interpretation is needed, document the question and recommend referral to the Bangsamoro Darul-Ifta (BDI), the region’s Islamic religious authority for juristic opinions (fatawa).

9.3.2 Scope: Exclusively Muslim

Section 1 of Article X states a clear boundary:6

Shari’ah shall apply exclusively to cases involving Muslims. Where a case involves a non-Muslim, Shari’ah law may apply only if the non-Muslim voluntarily submits to the jurisdiction of the Shari’ah court.

This is a hard limit. The researcher must confirm two elements:

  1. Are the parties Muslim? If all parties are Muslim, Shari’ah court jurisdiction may apply (depending on the subject matter). If no party is Muslim, Shari’ah does not apply.
  2. If a non-Muslim is involved, has that party voluntarily submitted? The non-Muslim must affirmatively consent. There is no deemed submission, no constructive submission. The consent must be voluntary.

Section 1 also protects the rights of non-Muslims: “The provisions of this Article shall not prejudice the rights of non-Muslims and nonindigenous peoples.”7

Research implication: Before diving into Shari’ah sources, confirm that the parties or subjects of your research question are Muslims (or that a non-Muslim has voluntarily submitted). If neither condition is met, Shari’ah does not apply to the question, and your research stays within the secular framework of Chapters 3-8.

9.3.3 The Three-Tiered Court System

The BOL establishes three levels of Shari’ah courts, each with defined jurisdiction. All three form part of the Philippine judicial system under Supreme Court supervision.8

Shari’ah Circuit Courts (Section 5) — the courts of first instance for most Shari’ah matters. They handle personal law disputes (marriage, divorce, dower, property, maintenance), minor offenses, communal property disputes, and civil actions within specified monetary thresholds.

Shari’ah District Courts (Section 6) — the intermediate courts. They handle custody, guardianship, legitimacy, estate settlement, corrections to Muslim Registries, customary and Shari’ah contracts, higher-value civil actions, and exercise appellate jurisdiction over Circuit Court decisions. They also exercise special powers: mandamus, prohibition, injunction, certiorari, and habeas corpus.9

Shari’ah High Court (Section 7) — the appellate court for the entire system. Composed of five justices including the Presiding Justice (Section 9). It exercises exclusive appellate jurisdiction over all District Court cases and exclusive original jurisdiction over annulment of District Court judgments and auxiliary writs. Its decisions are final and executory — except on questions of law, which may be elevated to the Supreme Court.10

The hierarchy of review:

Shari'ah Circuit Court
        ↓ (appeal)
Shari'ah District Court
        ↓ (appeal)
Shari'ah High Court
        ↓ (only on questions of law)
Supreme Court of the Philippines

Pending the complete organization of the Shari’ah High Court, the BOL provides that District Court decisions shall be appealable to the Court of Appeals.11 The researcher must verify the operational status of the Shari’ah High Court when assessing the current appellate pathway.

9.4 The Jurisdictional Matrix

This is the section you will consult most often. When your research identifies a Shari’ah dimension, the next question is: Which Shari’ah court has jurisdiction?

The answer depends on three factors: (1) the subject matter of the case, (2) the monetary value involved (for civil actions), and (3) the level of criminal penalty (for offenses). The following matrix consolidates the BOL’s jurisdictional grants.

Court LevelExclusive Original JurisdictionAppellate JurisdictionSpecial Powers
Shari'ah Circuit Court
(BOL Art. X, Sec. 5)		 • PD 1083 offenses committed in BARMM
• Marriage, divorce, betrothal, dower (mahr), property upon divorce, maintenance, restitution of marital rights
• Communal property disputes
• Ta'zir offenses punishable by arresto menor or corresponding fine
• Civil actions (real property: assessed value ≤ P400,000; other civil actions: demand ≤ P200,000) 		None None
Shari'ah District Court
(BOL Art. X, Sec. 6)		 • Custody, guardianship, legitimacy, paternity/filiation under PD 1083
• Estate settlement, probate of wills, letters of administration (regardless of property value)
• Muslim Registry corrections (Title VI, Book Two, PD 1083)
• Customary and Shari'ah-compliant contracts between Muslims
• Family home, change of name, insanity commitment
• Personal and real actions between Muslims (except forcible entry/unlawful detainer)
• Special civil actions (interpleader, declaratory relief) between Muslims
• Civil actions (real property: assessed value > P400,000; other civil actions: demand > P200,000) 		All Circuit Court cases within territorial jurisdiction Mandamus, prohibition, injunction, certiorari, habeas corpus
Shari'ah High Court
(BOL Art. X, Secs. 7, 9)		 • Annulment of District Court judgments
• Auxiliary writs in aid of appellate jurisdiction 		All District Court cases within or outside BARMM Mandamus, prohibition, injunction, certiorari, habeas corpus

How to Use the Matrix

Step 1: Identify the subject matter of the dispute or case in your research question.

Step 2: Match it against the exclusive original jurisdiction column. Start at the Circuit Court level. If the subject appears there, that is the court of first instance. If not, check the District Court level.

Step 3: For civil actions, check the monetary thresholds. Real property cases split at P400,000 (assessed value). Other civil actions split at P200,000 (demand or claim).

Step 4: For criminal matters, confirm the penalty level. Shari’ah courts have criminal jurisdiction ONLY over offenses punishable by arresto menor (imprisonment of one day to thirty days) or the ta’zir equivalent. This is a hard limit — Section 4 restricts criminal jurisdiction to “minor offenses punishable by arresto menor or ta’zir which must be equivalent to arresto menor or fines commensurate to the offense.”12

Step 5: Identify the appellate pathway. Circuit Court decisions go to the District Court. District Court decisions go to the High Court. High Court decisions are final — except on questions of law, which go to the Supreme Court.

9.5 PD 1083 and the BOL

Presidential Decree No. 1083, the Code of Muslim Personal Laws of the Philippines, was enacted on February 4, 1977 — more than four decades before the BOL. It remains the foundational codification of Muslim personal law in the Philippine legal system. Understanding the relationship between PD 1083 and the BOL is essential for Shari’ah research.

The Continuing Force of PD 1083

The BOL references PD 1083 repeatedly throughout Article X:

  • Section 5(a): Circuit Courts have jurisdiction over “offenses defined and punished under Presidential Decree No. 1083”13
  • Section 5(b): Circuit Courts handle disputes involving parties “married in accordance with Article 13 of Presidential Decree No. 1083”
  • Section 6(a): District Courts handle cases “arising under Presidential Decree No. 1083”
  • Section 6(c): District Courts handle Muslim Registry corrections “mentioned in Title VI of Book Two of Presidential Decree No. 1083”

These references confirm that PD 1083 is not superseded by the BOL. It continues to operate as the substantive law governing Muslim personal relations. The BOL builds upon it.

Parliament’s Supplementary Power

Section 4 grants the Parliament power to legislate in two directions:14

  1. Personal, family, and property law — Parliament can enact laws in these areas (subject to the Constitution and existing law)
  2. Commercial and civil actions not provided for under PD 1083 — Parliament can fill gaps that PD 1083 does not cover

The key phrase is “not provided for.” Where PD 1083 already covers a subject, it remains the governing law. Parliament’s power is supplementary — it addresses what PD 1083 left out, not what it already covers.

When PD 1083 and a Future BAA Overlap

If Parliament enacts a BAA that covers the same subject as PD 1083, three scenarios arise:

Scenario 1: The BAA supplements PD 1083. The BAA addresses a gap — a subject PD 1083 does not cover. Both laws coexist. Example: If Parliament enacts an Islamic finance BAA, it covers territory PD 1083 never addressed. No conflict.

Scenario 2: The BAA is consistent with PD 1083 but more detailed. The BAA provides additional procedural or substantive rules that do not contradict PD 1083. Both laws apply, with the BAA providing the additional detail. The national law relationship test determines whether the subject requires national law consistency or not.

Scenario 3: The BAA contradicts PD 1083. This is the hard case. PD 1083 is a national law (Tier 5). The BAA is a Bangsamoro law (Tier 4). The national law relationship test from Chapter 3 determines which prevails. If the subject is within the 55 enumerated powers and the BOL does not require national law consistency, the BAA governs within BARMM. If the BOL requires consistency with national law for the subject, PD 1083 prevails to the extent of the conflict.

Research Protocol for PD 1083

When your research question touches Muslim personal law:

  1. Start with PD 1083. It is the existing codification. Read the relevant title and articles.
  2. Check the BOL. Does Article X grant additional authority or modify the PD 1083 framework?
  3. Search for BAAs. Has Parliament enacted any legislation supplementing PD 1083?
  4. Check for pending bills. Is there legislation in the pipeline that would change the current framework?
  5. Build the Comparative Matrix. Place PD 1083, BOL Article X, any relevant BAA, and any applicable national law (e.g., the Family Code for mixed marriages) in adjacent columns.

9.6 Maqasid al-Shari’ah: The Objectives Framework

Beyond the specific jurisdictional rules, there is a broader framework for assessing whether legislation aligns with Islamic principles. The Maqasid al-Shari’ah — the higher objectives of Islamic law — provides that framework.

The Bangsamoro Shari’ah Legislation Framework proposes using Maqasid as the guiding philosophy for public policy: a “purpose-driven approach” that is “rational and universally accessible” and “compatible with the legal pluralism required by the BOL.”15 The five objectives are:

1. Protection of Faith (Din) Safeguarding religious freedom, practice, and worship. Legislation should not impede the free exercise of Islam. Example: A BAA regulating public events should not restrict Islamic religious gatherings.

2. Protection of Life (Nafs) Preserving life, health, and physical well-being. Legislation should advance the safety and health of persons. Example: A public health BAA aligns with this objective when it provides healthcare access for underserved Muslim communities.

3. Protection of Intellect (Aql) Safeguarding reason, education, and knowledge. Legislation should promote learning and prohibit what impairs rational capacity. Example: The Bangsamoro Education Code (BAA 18) advances this objective by establishing the educational framework for the region, including madrasah education.

4. Protection of Lineage (Nasl) Preserving family, progeny, and social bonds. Legislation should strengthen the family unit and protect the welfare of children. Example: PD 1083’s provisions on marriage, divorce, and custody serve this objective.

5. Protection of Property (Mal) Safeguarding wealth, economic rights, and fair transactions. Legislation should protect property rights and ensure just economic dealings. Example: A future Islamic finance BAA would advance this objective by establishing Shari’ah-compliant financial instruments.

How Researchers Use Maqasid

The Maqasid framework is not a source of binding law — no court will strike down a BAA solely for violating Maqasid. But it serves three research functions:

First, as a policy alignment test. When researching the legal basis for proposed legislation, assess which Maqasid objective(s) the proposal advances. A bill that protects none of the five objectives raises a red flag — it may not align with Islamic principles, even if it is technically within Parliament’s jurisdiction.

Second, as a legislative intent framework. When analyzing the purpose of an existing BAA that has a Shari’ah dimension, the Maqasid framework helps articulate the underlying policy rationale in Islamic terms.

Third, as a comparative lens. When comparing BARMM legislation with Islamic law frameworks in other jurisdictions (Malaysia, Indonesia, Brunei), the Maqasid framework provides a common analytical vocabulary.

Add a Maqasid Assessment row to your Comparative Matrix whenever the research question involves Shari’ah. Identify which of the five objectives are engaged and how the legal provisions under review advance (or fail to advance) them.

9.7 Nested Sovereignty and Legal Pluralism

To research Shari’ah effectively in BARMM, you need to understand the structural model within which it operates. The Bangsamoro Shari’ah Legislation Framework describes this as “nested sovereignty” — the BARMM exercises “culturally-specific sovereignty that is nested within, and ultimately subordinate to, the national sovereignty of the Philippine Republic.”16

Three principles follow from this model:

9.7.1 Constitutional Supremacy

All Shari’ah legislation must comply with the 1987 Constitution. The Constitution is the supreme law. The BOL, which authorizes Shari’ah courts and Shari’ah legislation, is itself subject to the Constitution. A BAA implementing Shari’ah provisions cannot violate constitutional rights — due process, equal protection, freedom of religion, or any other constitutional guarantee.

Research implication: When assessing proposed Shari’ah legislation, always check for constitutional compatibility. A provision may be consistent with Islamic law but unconstitutional. The Constitution prevails.

9.7.2 Judicial Integration

Shari’ah courts are part of the Philippine judicial system, not separate from it. Section 2 of Article X states this directly: “Shari’ah courts within the Bangsamoro territorial jurisdiction shall form part of the Philippine judicial system subject to the supervision of the Supreme Court.”17

The Supreme Court: - Supervises all Shari’ah courts (Section 2) - Promulgates the Shari’ah Rules of Court (Section 11) - Administers the Shari’ah Special Bar Examinations (Section 12) - Appoints and disciplines Shari’ah court personnel (Section 14) - Reviews Shari’ah High Court decisions on questions of law (Section 7)

Research implication: When searching for Shari’ah jurisprudence, the Supreme Court E-Library is your primary database for appellate decisions. Shari’ah court rulings that have been appealed on questions of law appear in the SC’s case database.

9.7.3 Legal Pluralism

Three legal traditions coexist in BARMM: - The Philippine Constitution and national statutory law — the overarching framework - Shari’ah — Islamic law applicable to Muslims - Traditional or tribal law — customary law applicable to indigenous peoples

These three are not competitors. They apply to different populations and different subject matters, with the Constitution as the ceiling for all. The researcher’s task is to determine which tradition (or combination) applies to a given question — and how they interact when they overlap.

9.8 Researching Traditional and Tribal Law

Article X does not address only Shari’ah. Sections 17-18 establish the framework for traditional and tribal justice.

9.8.1 The BOL Framework

Section 1 states that “traditional or tribal laws shall be applicable to disputes of indigenous peoples within the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region.”18

Section 17 directs Parliament to “enact laws to promote and support the traditional or tribal justice systems that are appropriate for the indigenous peoples.” It recognizes the right of indigenous peoples to “use their own commonly accepted justice systems, conflict resolution institutions, peace building processes or mechanisms, and other customary laws and practices within their respective communities and as may be compatible with the national legal system and with internationally recognized human rights.”19

Section 18 creates the Office for Tribal Justice System to oversee the study, preservation, and development of tribal justice within BARMM.20

9.8.2 The IP Rights Anchor

BOL Article IX, Section 3 states that rights under RA 8371 (Indigenous Peoples’ Rights Act, or IPRA) “shall not be diminished.”21 This means that any BARMM legislation affecting indigenous peoples must be consistent with IPRA protections. The Ministry of Indigenous Peoples’ Affairs (MIPA) has institutional jurisdiction over IP-related matters.

9.8.3 The Research Challenge: Uncodified Law

Traditional law is fundamentally different from statutory law. Most tribal and customary practices are uncodified — they exist as oral traditions, community practices, and decisions by tribal councils, not as written legislation.

This creates a unique research challenge. You cannot search for traditional law the way you search for a BAA or a Supreme Court decision. The research methods include:

  1. Consult MIPA records. MIPA may maintain documentation of customary practices for specific indigenous groups within BARMM.
  2. Consult the Office for Tribal Justice System. If operational, this office is mandated to study and document tribal justice systems.
  3. Review IPRA documentation. RA 8371 and its IRR describe processes for recognizing customary law, particularly in the context of ancestral domain claims and community governance.
  4. Consult community leaders. For specific questions about customary practice, the authoritative sources may be tribal elders, datus, or council leaders — not written documents.
  5. Check academic sources. Ethnographic studies, university research, and publications by organizations like NCIP (National Commission on Indigenous Peoples) may document customary practices.

Documentation standard: When your research relies on traditional or customary law that is not codified in any written source, mark it clearly: [UNVERIFIED — based on customary practice, not codified law]. This notation alerts the reader that the legal basis rests on community practice rather than enacted legislation, and that independent verification with community leaders may be needed.

9.9 Where to Find Shari’ah Sources

9.9.1 Primary Legal Sources

Source Where to Find It What It Contains
BOL Article X Republic Act No. 11054, Article X (The Justice System in the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region) 20 sections: Shari’ah courts, jurisdiction, sources of law, judges, tribal justice, ADR
PD 1083 LawPhil Project (lawphil.net); Official Gazette Code of Muslim Personal Laws: marriage, divorce, inheritance, property, family relations
Shari’ah court decisions SC E-Library (elibrary.judiciary.gov.ph) for cases appealed on questions of law Shari’ah High Court decisions elevated to the Supreme Court
BAAs with Shari’ah dimension Bangsamoro Parliament archives; local repository at legislation/BAAs/ Any enacted BAA touching on Muslim personal law, Islamic finance, halal, waqf, or zakat
Executive issuances Official Gazette of the Bangsamoro; BARMM agency websites MCs, AOs, and EOs implementing Shari’ah-related provisions

9.9.2 Institutional Sources

Institution Role What It Provides
Bangsamoro Darul-Ifta (BDI) Regional Islamic religious authority Juristic opinions (fatawa) on Islamic law questions; guidance on Shari’ah compliance
Supreme Court Supervises all Shari’ah courts Rules of Court for Shari’ah proceedings; appellate decisions; appointment of judges
Ministry of Indigenous Peoples’ Affairs (MIPA) Institutional jurisdiction over IP matters Documentation of customary practices; coordination with tribal leaders
Office for Tribal Justice System Study and preservation of tribal justice Documentation and development of tribal justice systems within BARMM

9.9.3 International and Comparative Sources

When your research requires comparative analysis with other Islamic legal systems, these sources are relevant:

  • AAOIFI Standards (Accounting and Auditing Organization for Islamic Financial Institutions) — international standards for Islamic finance, banking, and insurance
  • OIC Fiqh Academy Resolutions — juristic opinions from the Organisation of Islamic Cooperation’s International Islamic Fiqh Academy on contemporary legal questions
  • Malaysian Shari’ah court decisions — Malaysia operates a dual-track legal system (civil and Shari’ah) similar in some respects to BARMM’s legal pluralism
  • Indonesian Islamic law framework — the world’s largest Muslim-majority country with a codified Kompilasi Hukum Islam (Compilation of Islamic Law) for personal matters
  • Academic journals — Journal of Islamic Law and Culture, Islamic Law and Society, Journal of Muslim Minority Affairs

Caution: International sources provide comparative context, not binding authority. The BARMM Shari’ah framework is defined by the Philippine Constitution, the BOL, and PD 1083. A fatwa from the OIC Fiqh Academy or a Malaysian court ruling may be persuasive but is never controlling in a Philippine legal context. Always cite international sources as comparative references, not as primary authority.

9.10 Worked Example

Research question: What is the legal framework governing Muslim inheritance in BARMM?

Step 1: Issue Analysis

Apply the Three-Part Frame from Chapter 2:

Element Answer
Subject Muslim inheritance (succession)
Jurisdiction Both BARMM and national — personal law for Muslims, but estate tax is national
Action Map the complete legal framework: who has authority, what law governs distribution, what law governs taxation

Shari’ah dimension? Yes — inheritance is a core area of Muslim personal law, explicitly within Shari’ah court jurisdiction.

Step 2: National Law Relationship

Is inheritance within the 55 enumerated powers? Article V, Section 2 includes (a) “Administration of justice” and multiple powers related to personal law. Article X, Section 4 grants Parliament power over “personal, family, and property law.” Result: YES.

Does the BOL require consistency with national law? No specific provision in Article X requires consistency with national tax law for inheritance distribution. However, national tax law (estate tax under the NIRC) is a residual national power — the BOL does not grant BARMM the power to impose or exempt estate tax. Result: The subject straddles two relationships to national law. The distribution of the estate (who inherits what share) is within Bangsamoro jurisdiction (no national law consistency required). The taxation of the estate transfer is a residual national power.

Step 3: Systematic Source Search

Tier 1 — Constitution: Article III (Bill of Rights) protects property rights. Article X, Section 20 authorizes autonomous regions to legislate on “personal, family, and property relations.”

Tier 2 — BOL: Article X, Section 4 grants Parliament power over personal and property law. Article X, Section 6 gives the Shari’ah District Court exclusive original jurisdiction over “disposition, distribution, and settlement of the estate of deceased Muslims, probate of wills, issuance of letters of administration, or appointment of administrators or executors regardless of the nature or the aggregate value of the property.”22

Tier 4 — BAAs: Search the BAA archive for any enacted legislation on inheritance or succession. As of March 2026: none found.

Tier 5 — National law: - PD 1083, Title II (Succession): This is the existing codification of Muslim inheritance law. It prescribes the shares of heirs according to Islamic law (faraid). - National Internal Revenue Code (NIRC), as amended by RA 10963 (TRAIN Law): Estate tax of six percent (6%) on the net estate above P5,000,000. This applies to all decedents regardless of religion.

Tier 7 — Jurisprudence: Search the SC E-Library for decisions on Shari’ah court jurisdiction over estate settlement and for any rulings on the interaction between PD 1083 and the Family Code in inheritance matters.

Step 4: Comparative Matrix

AspectBOL (RA 11054)PD 1083NIRC (Estate Tax)Shari'ah Principles
What It Governs Jurisdictional authority — which court handles the estate Substantive law — who inherits what share Taxation — how much tax is owed on the transfer The underlying principles of equitable distribution (faraid)
Key Provisions Art. X, Sec. 6(b): Shari'ah District Court has exclusive jurisdiction over estate settlement regardless of value Title II: prescribes legal heirs, compulsory shares, and rules of distribution for Muslims Sec. 84-97 (as amended): 6% estate tax on net estate exceeding P5M Qur'anic verses on inheritance (e.g., Surah An-Nisa 4:11-12); fixed shares for designated heirs
Applies To All estate cases where the decedent is Muslim Muslims; Family Code applies if the decedent is non-Muslim All decedents, regardless of religion Muslims; the religious basis for PD 1083's inheritance rules
Interaction PD 1083 governs DISTRIBUTION (the shares). NIRC governs TAXATION (the rate). The BOL determines JURISDICTION (which court). All three apply simultaneously in their respective domains. Shari'ah principles inform the interpretation of PD 1083's inheritance provisions.
Maqasid Protection of Property (Mal) — ensuring equitable distribution of wealth across generations. Protection of Lineage (Nasl) — preserving family bonds and intergenerational obligations.

Step 5: Research Answer

Muslim inheritance in BARMM is governed by three laws operating simultaneously:

  1. PD 1083 (Title II) provides the substantive rules — the prescribed shares for each category of heir under Islamic law.
  2. BOL Article X, Section 6(b) vests exclusive jurisdiction in the Shari’ah District Court for estate settlement, regardless of the aggregate value of the property.
  3. The NIRC (as amended by TRAIN Law) imposes estate tax on the net estate — this is a national tax law that applies regardless of the religious affiliation of the decedent.

No BAA has been enacted on Muslim inheritance as of March 2026. If Parliament enacts one, Article X, Section 4 limits its scope to matters “not provided for” under PD 1083 — it can supplement PD 1083 but should not contradict it. The Shari’ah principles underlying PD 1083’s inheritance rules are rooted in the Maqasid of protecting property (Mal) and lineage (Nasl).

9.11 Common Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Treating Shari’ah as a separate tier. Shari’ah is not Tier 8. It is a parallel dimension that intersects all seven tiers. When you isolate Shari’ah from the hierarchy, you miss the interactions — how the Constitution constrains it, how the BOL frames it, how national law coexists with it. Always research Shari’ah within the seven-tier framework, not outside it.

Pitfall 2: Assuming Parliament can expand Shari’ah criminal jurisdiction beyond arresto menor. Article X, Section 4 limits criminal jurisdiction to “minor offenses punishable by arresto menor or ta’zir which must be equivalent to arresto menor or fines commensurate to the offense.”23 A bill imposing imprisonment beyond arresto menor (one day to thirty days) through the Shari’ah court framework exceeds the BOL’s grant. The researcher must flag any proposed legislation that attempts to expand beyond this limit.

Pitfall 3: Applying Shari’ah to non-Muslims. Section 1 states that Shari’ah applies “exclusively to cases involving Muslims.” A non-Muslim is subject to Shari’ah jurisdiction only upon voluntary submission. No law, BAA, or executive issuance can extend Shari’ah jurisdiction to non-Muslims without their consent. If your research question involves non-Muslim parties who have not submitted to Shari’ah jurisdiction, the Shari’ah framework does not apply.

Pitfall 4: Interpreting Shari’ah sources independently. The legal researcher identifies when Shari’ah applies, maps the jurisdictional framework, and locates the applicable provisions. The researcher does not independently interpret Quranic verses or Hadith to derive legal rules. That is the domain of Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh) and should be referred to the Bangsamoro Darul-Ifta or qualified Islamic legal scholars. Overstepping this boundary produces unreliable research.

Pitfall 5: Ignoring PD 1083. PD 1083 is nearly five decades old, but it remains the primary codification of Muslim personal law. The BOL builds on it rather than replacing it. Researchers who skip PD 1083 and jump straight to the BOL miss the substantive law — the BOL provides jurisdictional authority, but PD 1083 provides the rules.

Pitfall 6: Conflating traditional/tribal law with Shari’ah. Traditional law and Shari’ah are distinct legal traditions. Shari’ah derives from Islamic religious sources. Traditional law derives from indigenous customs and practices. They apply to different populations — Shari’ah to Muslims, traditional law to indigenous peoples. Sections 17-18 of Article X address traditional law separately from Sections 1-16 on Shari’ah. Do not treat them as interchangeable.

Pitfall 7: Citing international Islamic sources as binding authority. AAOIFI standards, OIC Fiqh Academy resolutions, and comparative Shari’ah jurisprudence from other countries are persuasive but not binding. The BARMM Shari’ah framework is defined by Philippine law — the Constitution, the BOL, and PD 1083. Always cite the Philippine sources as primary authority and international sources as comparative context.

9.12 Template: Shari’ah Applicability Matrix

Use this template at the start of any research task that may have a Shari’ah dimension. It is also provided as a detachable worksheet in Appendix H.

QuestionAnalysisResult
1. Does the subject involve Muslims?
(Parties, beneficiaries, or regulated persons)		[Identify the parties and their religious affiliation]YES / NO
2. Does the subject fall within Shari'ah court jurisdiction?

3. Does PD 1083 cover this subject?
(Marriage, divorce, inheritance, family relations, property)		[Identify the relevant PD 1083 title and articles]YES / NO
4. Has Parliament enacted a BAA on this subject?
(Search BAA archive)		[BAA number and title, or "None found"]YES / NO

Shari’ah Research Checklist

After completing your research on a question with a Shari’ah dimension, verify that you have:

  • ☐ Confirmed whether the parties or subjects are Muslim (Section 9.3.2)
  • ☐ Checked BOL Article X for jurisdictional authority (Section 9.3.3)
  • ☐ Searched PD 1083 for applicable substantive provisions (Section 9.5)
  • ☐ Searched the BAA archive for Shari’ah-related legislation (Section 9.5)
  • ☐ Applied the Jurisdictional Matrix to determine the correct court (Section 9.4)
  • ☐ Confirmed that any criminal provisions do not exceed arresto menor (Section 9.4, Step 4)
  • ☐ Checked the SC E-Library for Shari’ah-related jurisprudence (Section 9.9)
  • ☐ Identified which Maqasid objectives are engaged (Section 9.6)
  • ☐ Checked for a traditional/tribal law dimension (Section 9.8)
  • ☐ Marked any uncodified customary law as [UNVERIFIED] (Section 9.8.3)
  • ☐ Cited international sources as comparative only, not binding (Section 9.9.3)
  • ☐ Added a Shari’ah column to the Comparative Matrix
  • ☐ Flagged any questions requiring referral to BDI or Islamic legal scholars

9.13 Quality Checklist

Before relying on your Shari’ah research, confirm:

  • ☐ The Shari’ah Applicability Matrix has been completed for this research question
  • ☐ All BOL Article X provisions cited have been verified against the verbatim source text
  • ☐ PD 1083 provisions have been confirmed as current (check for amendments)
  • ☐ The Jurisdictional Matrix has been applied correctly — subject matter and monetary thresholds match the correct court level
  • ☐ The criminal jurisdiction limit (arresto menor) has been verified for any criminal law question
  • ☐ The scope limitation (exclusively Muslim, voluntary submission for non-Muslims) has been confirmed
  • ☐ The Shari’ah column has been added to the Comparative Matrix
  • ☐ No Quranic verses or Hadith have been independently interpreted without referral to qualified Islamic scholars
  • ☐ Traditional/tribal law has been distinguished from Shari’ah where both are relevant
  • ☐ All uncodified customary law is marked [UNVERIFIED — based on customary practice]
  • ☐ International Islamic law sources are cited as comparative, not binding
  • ☐ The national law relationship test from Chapter 3 has been applied to determine which tier controls
  • ☐ Constitutional compatibility has been assessed for any proposed Shari’ah legislation

Key Takeaways

  1. Shari’ah is parallel, not separate. It intersects all seven tiers of the legal hierarchy. Research it within the seven-tier framework using the same five-step methodology.

  2. Three laws govern most Shari’ah questions simultaneously. The BOL provides jurisdictional authority. PD 1083 provides the substantive rules. The Constitution provides the ceiling. Apply all three.

  3. Know the hard limits. Shari’ah applies exclusively to Muslims. Criminal jurisdiction stops at arresto menor. The Supreme Court has final authority on questions of law. Parliament legislates on matters not covered by PD 1083.

  4. Researchers map and locate — they do not interpret. Your role is to identify when Shari’ah applies, which court has jurisdiction, and what the codified law says. Interpretation of Islamic primary sources belongs to qualified Islamic scholars and the Bangsamoro Darul-Ifta.

  5. Traditional law is not Shari’ah. They are distinct traditions with distinct sources, distinct populations, and distinct constitutional bases. Research them separately.

  6. Always complete the Shari’ah Applicability Matrix. It ensures you do not miss a Shari’ah dimension — and it ensures you do not create one where none exists.
